# Habitat for Coilia nasus in southern Zhejiang Province, China, based on a maximum entropy model

## Abstract
As an important fishery resource and endangered species, studying the habitat of Coilia nasus (C. nasus) is highly significant. This study used fishery survey data from southern Zhejiang coastal waters from 2016 to 2020, employing a maximum entropy model (MaxEnt) to map the habitat distribution of C. nasus. Model performance was evaluated using two metrics: the area under the curve (AUC) of the receiver operating characteristic curve for the training and test sets and true skill statistics (TSS). This study aimed to predict the habitat distribution of C. nasus and explore how environmental variables influence habitat suitability. The results indicated that the models for each season had strong predictive performance, with AUC values above 0.8 and TSS values exceeding 0.6, indicating that they could accurately predict the presence of C. nasus. In the study area, C. nasus was primarily found in brackish or marine waters near bays and coastal islands. Among all environmental factors, salinity (S) and bottom temperature (BOT) had the highest correlations with habitat distribution, although these correlations varied across seasons. The findings of this study provide empirical evidence and a reference for the conservation and management of C. nasus and for the designation of its protected areas.

## Introduction
Species distribution models (SDMs), which are commonly used in theoretical and applied research in ecology and biogeography, have seen advances in application in recent years, as computer processing power and the amount of available environmental and species distribution data have increased. The most common applications are the simulation of suitable habitats for species and conservation planning. Each of these applications relies on the model having good predictive performance.

SDMs are widely used for marine species, with fish being the most frequently studied group. Thanks to commercial fishing activities, data from commercial catches are available, leading to the thorough assessment and management of commercial species. In contrast, it is difficult to obtain reliable data for rare or protected species in complex marine environments, making the application of SDMs less common, despite the critical importance of conserving and managing marine species. Factors such as climate change and human pressures are accelerating changes in the geographical distribution of species. Identifying the spatial distribution of fish is crucial for population assessment and management, with habitat distribution prediction being a particularly important aspect.

C. nasus, a coastal migratory fish, inhabits the northwest Pacific regions of China, Japan, and Korea. In China, it is mainly found in the Bohai Sea, Yellow Sea, East China Sea, and the middle and lower reaches of the rivers that flow into these seas. Based on reproductive habits and morphological characteristics, C. nasus can be categorized into three ecological types: anadromous, freshwater resident, and freshwater landlocked. The anadromous type spawns in rivers and grows in the sea, while the freshwater resident and landlocked types live their entire lives in freshwater habitats. Factors such as water pollution, intensive fishing, destruction of juvenile fish resources, and barriers to migration caused by hydroelectric projects have led to habitat degradation, significant population decline, and loss of ecological function for C. nasus. As an important economic fish in China, C. nasus was listed as "Endangered (EN)" on the International Union for the Conservation of Nature (IUCN) Red List in 2018. Understanding the habitat preferences and spatiotemporal distribution of C. nasus is crucial for its conservation and management, as well as for ecosystem restoration. Current research on C. nasus mainly focuses on feeding habits, habitat history, and growth characteristics, with a lack of direct studies on its habitat.

SDMs can accurately predict potential habitats by quantifying the relationships between a species spatial distribution and a set of environmental variables. The maximum entropy model (MaxEnt) is one of the most commonly used methods for studying species distributions. This model can better describe the complex relationships between species habitats and the environment than traditional modeling approaches. As a machine learning method, MaxEnt does not require assumptions concerning the relationship between predictor variables and species distributions, and thus it provides flexibility in describing complex relationships among predictive factors. When data are limited, MaxEnt is considered effective, as it is capable of using partial data to model species distributions.

Therefore, this study employed habitat suitability modeling based on the MaxEnt model to assess the potential impact of seasonal environmental variables on the spatiotemporal distribution of C. nasus. The goal of this work was to analyze and map the seasonal habitat preferences and distribution of C. nasus in southern Zhejiang. The results provide a reference for establishing protected areas and for the sustainable use, conservation, and management of C. nasus populations.

## Materials and methods
### Fishery data
The MaxEnt model used in this study only requires presence records of C. nasus. Species data were obtained from fishery resource surveys conducted during different seasons from 2016 to 2020. The survey areas included estuaries, bays, and coastal waters, and the survey vessels used bottom trawling. A total of 248 stations were set up within the range of 26°30′N–29°30′N and 120°E–123°E and 273 C. nasus specimens were found (Fig. 1). All records were assigned to one of four seasons (Table 1) based on the survey dates. The fishery record data included information on the operation date, net deployment and retrieval positions, trawling duration, mesh size, and the biological characteristics of the catch.

### Environmental data
When selecting environmental variables, we considered the biological characteristics of C. nasus, its ecological type (warm temperate, coastal migratory), and information from previous studies. We chose sea water temperature at the sea floor (BOT), chlorophyll-a concentration (CHL), dissolved oxygen (DO), ocean mixed layer depth (MLD), sea surface height (SSH), sea water salinity (S), and sea surface temperature (SST) as variables for our habitat model to predict the spatiotemporal distribution of C. nasus. All marine environmental data were downloaded from the COPERNICUS website. We used seasonal average data from 2016 to 2020 and coupled it with species occurrence data.

### Modeling with MaxEnt
MaxEnt is one of the most widely used modeling techniques for simulating species distributions, especially for predicting species distributions from partial data. MaxEnt was designed to work with presence-only data and thus requires relatively little information. MaxEnt has a robust prediction accuracy, even with small sample sizes. These characteristics made it suitable for modeling the relatively small amount of fishery survey data in this study to accurately provide species occurrence records and biological information.

**Treatment of environmental variables and sampling bias correction.** In this study, environmental variables were temporally divided into winter (January–March), spring (April–June), summer (July–September), and autumn (October–December). The variance inflation factor (VIF) was used to avoid multicollinearity, with all VIF values being less than 10. Monthly average data of different environmental variables were processed in ArcMap to calculate seasonal averages for model coupling. For MaxEnt, all input data must have the same resolution to ensure compatibility. Using the resampling function in ArcMap, the spatial resolution of environmental data was standardized to 0.05° × 0.05° for model inclusion. The importance of each environmental factor was analyzed based on its contribution rate.

MaxEnt modeling operates under the assumption of systematic or random unbiased sampling of the study area, which is often difficult to achieve in practice. Furthermore, MaxEnt is susceptible to sampling bias, and thus it requires bias correction for species data. In this study, the most common spatial filtering method was used. ENMTools was employed to retain species data points based on the spatial scale of environmental variables. Additionally, background sampling can reduce spatial bias and model uncertainty. Therefore, this study used ArcGIS software to restrict MaxEnt background sampling within a 95% kernel density contour of all occurrence data, and a bias file was created to enhance the model's accuracy.

**MaxEnt modeling solutions.** Modeling was performed using MAXENT 3.4.1 software, and 75% of the records of occurrence of C. nasus samples were randomly chosen as the training set each time, while the remaining 25% of the occurrence data points were used as the test set. In this study, MaxEnt's feature parameter options included Linear features, Quadratic features, Product features, and Hinge features. Random seed was selected in the base setting; the regularization multiplier parameter was set to 1, and for the Repeat run mode we chose subsample and repeated the calculation 100 times for each month. For model output, we chose Logistic as the result output format.

**Evaluation of model performance.** We evaluated the predictive ability of MaxEnt using the size of the area under the receiver operating characteristic curve (AUC), which is the most commonly used method for assessing SDM performance. This metric quantifies the model's ability to distinguish between actual species occurrence points and pseudo-absences, with an AUC value closer to 1 indicating a higher predictive accuracy. As a modeling method based solely on presence data, specific thresholds can also be derived from logistic output to assess the model's performance. Determining thresholds also involves considering the relative importance of both omission and commission errors. True skill statistics (TSS) is a widely accepted metric for evaluating both types of errors. TSS values range from 0 to 1, with higher values indicating better model performance. In this study, AUC and TSS values from both the test and training sets were used as indicators to evaluate the overall performance of the model.

**Correlations of environmental variables.** In this study, the contribution ratio provided by the software was chosen to measure the correlations of environmental factors. In this system, the correlations of environmental factors are measured by the percentage of contribution rate produced by the model, and the larger the ratio, the greater the correlation. The environmental factors with higher correlations were selected to plot response curves as well as to plot the distributions of the environmental factors with the highest correlations in each season to compare with the predicted habitat distribution results.

**Mapping of habitat suitability distributions.** MaxEnt's logistic outputs can be interpreted as the probability of the presence of the target species, and when MaxEnt's outputs are used in qualitative analytical explorations, they can be interpreted as a habitat suitability index (HSI). This index ranges from 0 to 1, with values close to 1 indicating higher habitat suitability. The spatial and temporal patterns of the HSI distribution of C. nasus were plotted and compared according to the outputs of the habitat model in different seasons to visualize and analyze the changes in the distribution characteristics of the habitat for C. nasus in each season.

## Results
### Evaluation of the model's predictive performance
The AUC values for both the training and testing datasets in each season were above 0.8. This indicated that the selected environmental variables played significant roles in habitat selection for C. nasus. The habitat suitability simulation results demonstrated high discriminatory ability, suggesting that the model was adequate for studying the distribution of suitable habitats for C. nasus in southern Zhejiang. Furthermore, the TSS values for each season were high, indicating good model performance.

### Contributions of environmental variables to the model
The percentage contributions of the environmental factors in the MaxEnt model for each season indicated that the seasons showed a pattern in which the correlation of a single factor was the most prominent. Among winter, spring, and fall, S had the highest contribution, with values all higher than 60%, while in summer BOT had the highest correlation to the distribution of habitat suitability for the knife crack, also above 60%.

### Spatial and temporal distribution for C. nasus
Regarding habitat suitability for C. nasus, the distribution was the widest in winter, after which the habitat range gradually contracted, becoming narrowest in summer, and then expanding in autumn. However, the distribution of the most suitable habitat was roughly the same in all seasons, being primarily in brackish or marine waters such as Yueqing Bay, Dongtou, and the Yuhuan Outer Sea.

### Response curves and optimal ranges of environmental variables
In this study, the two environmental factors with the highest relevance to the distribution of suitable habitat for C. nasus were selected to plot response curves. In addition, the actual distributions of the factors with the highest correlations with habitat suitability were plotted for each season. In the seasonal salinity trends, there were clear peaks in winter and summer, with the highest values of the habitat suitability index at salinities of 22 and 32, respectively; the habitat suitability in spring and autumn showed decreasing trends with increasing salinity.

For the seafloor temperature, the habitat suitability for C. nasus gradually increased in winter up to 13°C, reaching the highest value between 13 and 17°C and remaining stable, and then decreasing with increasing temperature; in spring and autumn, there were single peaks, with the highest suitability at 21°C and 28°C respectively; in autumn, the suitability continued to increase with the temperature, reaching the highest at 27°C and then remaining stable.

The highest correlations with habitat suitability for C. nasus in each season were for salinity in winter, spring, and autumn, but there were differences in the extent of the influence on the habitat suitability. Compared with the seasonal distribution of C. nasus habitat suitability, we found that in winter, C. nasus was largely distributed in waters with salinity <30, and the salinity of the highly suitable distribution area ranged from 14 to 18; in spring, C. nasus was also largely distributed in waters with salinity <30, and the salinity of the highly suitable distribution area ranged from 24 to 28. In autumn, the salinity trend was the most significant, and the main distribution area for C. nasus was in the sea, with salinity <30, and the salinity in the highly suitable distribution area was between 16–20. In summer, the correlation of seafloor temperature was the most important, with the main distribution area of C. nasus being in the sea area with a temperature >26°C, and the temperature in the highly suitable distribution area ranged from 28 to 30°C.

## Discussion
### The maximum entropy model and its applicability for rare species
All of the models used in this study achieved AUC values above 0.8, indicating that they had good overall predictive performance. The TSS values were also high, indicating low error rates in predicting the presence of the species. SDMs are reliable tools for predicting species distributions and hence guiding conservation and management efforts. The IUCN has employed SDMs to assess the range of extinction risk for species. The reliability of SDMs is influenced by data availability, appropriate predictor variable selection, and model accuracy. For this study, despite the catch data for C. nasus across seasons being highly credible survey data, the information generally covered a limited range in terms of time and space. This is particularly true for rare or scarce species, where records often only indicate occurrence. Such incomplete distribution information poses challenges for evaluation and assessment. Partial data simulation is necessary to model species distributions under such conditions of limited data. In such cases, MaxEnt is considered a viable alternative, at times performing better than traditional methods. MaxEnt, as a machine learning method using "presence-only" data, has been widely applied in habitat modeling for various marine taxa, and it remains the most common approach. MaxEnt excels in modeling distributions of rare species. Based on the evaluation criteria for our model, the habitat distribution results for C. nasus in the southern waters of Zhejiang generated by MaxEnt are highly reliable and can support the delineation of conservation areas for this species.

### Habitat utilization and environmental preferences of C. nasus
The coastal waters of southern Zhejiang are part of the central-southern core of the East China Sea. Influenced by the Zhejiang Coastal Current, Taiwan Warm Current, and Kuroshio Current, this region boasts excellent hydrological conditions, abundant nutrients, and plentiful food organisms, creating an ideal habitat for C. nasus. The spatial and temporal distribution of C. nasus in this region is relatively uniform, primarily concentrated in bays and shallow areas near islands and the coast. This may be due to the complex seabed topography and diverse geology between the islands, along with the rich hydrological environment, which supports various fish species. The habitat range of C. nasus gradually decreases following winter, reaching its lowest extent in summer and then expanding significantly in autumn. The summer habitat range is narrower compared to the other three seasons, which is consistent with the findings of Liu et al. regarding the spatiotemporal niche of C. nasus in this region. This may be due to reproductive migration. Coilia nasus in the Oujiang Estuary is a warm-temperate migratory species that prefers warmer waters. The species exhibits homing behavior in fixed river channels during the reproductive migration. Several rivers are present in this region, with the Oujiang River being the largest and the primary reproductive migration base. Coilia nasus in the Oujiang Estuary begin migrating upriver into the Oujiang from March to April and then return to the sea for overwintering when the water temperatures drop in October. This seasonal pattern aligns with the study's findings concerning the changes in the habitat range of C. nasus.

Except for in summer, the habitat distribution of C. nasus in this study is closely related to seawater salinity. This matches the ecological habits of migratory C. nasus, as they mainly inhabit areas with lower salinity. Juvenile C. nasus hatch and feed in low-salinity environments before moving to higher-salinity areas for overwintering. In the Oujiang Estuary, C. nasus may be ecologically isolated from those in the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, but their growth characteristics are similar to those of the Qiantang River population. Similar to the Qiantang River C. nasus, the species in our study area spends a short life stage in freshwater, only spawning and breeding there. Most of their time is spent in brackish or marine waters, and they can migrate flexibly between these environments.

### Limitations and prospects
This study uses fishery survey data and environmental data to model and analyze the habitat distribution and environmental preferences of C. nasus in the southern Zhejiang sea area. However, the limited sample size and data coverage, particularly across different seasons and regions, may affect the representativeness and generalizability of the results. Otohith microchemistry habitat histories reveal that the C. nasus population in the Oujiang River area includes freshwater-estuarine brackish water and freshwater-estuarine brackish water-marine types, each with different life stages. This study, however, only depicts the habitat distribution of C. nasus, and it lacks detailed descriptions of migration paths and behavior patterns between different water bodies and growth stages, information that is crucial for developing conservation and management strategies. Therefore, future research should consider these factors. Additionally, there are differences in environmental preferences and feeding habits between adult and juvenile fish. Considering the distribution of C. nasus at different life stages may be more meaningful for resource conservation and management and provide a scientific basis for these efforts.

---

# Projected habitat preferences of commercial fish under different scenarios of climate change

## Abstract
The challenges of commercial species with the threats of climate change make it necessary to predict the changes in the distributional shifts and habitat preferences of the species under possible future scenarios. We aim to demonstrate how future climatic changes will affect the habitat suitability of three species of commercial fish using the predictive technique MaxEnt. The dataset used to extract geographical records included OBIS (54%), GBIF (1%), and literature (45%). The output of the model indicated accurate projections of MaxEnt (AUC above 0.9). Temperature was the main descriptor responsible for the main effects on the distribution of commercial fish. With increasing RCP from 2.5 to 8.5, the species would prefer saltier, higher temperatures and deeper waters in the future. We observed different percentages of suitable habitats between species during RCPs showing distinct sensitivity of each fish in facing climate changes. Negative effects from climate change on the distribution patterns of commercial fish were predicted to lead to varying degrees of reduction and changes of suitable habitats and movement of species towards higher latitudes. The finding emphasizes to implement adaptive management measures to preserve the stocks of these commercial fish considering that the intensification of the effects of climate change on subtropical areas and overexploited species is predicted.

## Introduction
The marine ecosystem is predicted to face an unknown challenge of climate change in the future. Marine taxa response to climate changes through pole-ward and depth shifts. It was projected the average global ocean temperature will increase up to 2°C by the end of this century that depending on different scenarios of greenhouse gas concentrations. Representative Concentration Pathways (RCPs) represent alternative greenhouse gas concentration routes (2.6, 4.5, 6.0, 8.5 W/m² by 2100). In RCP 2.6, an initial increase in temperature until 2020 followed by a decline. RCP4.5 and RCP6.0 are known as two intermediate scenarios and finally, in RCP 8.5, a very marked temperature increase is predicted throughout the twenty-first century. Greenhouse gas emissions have a main role in future changes of environmental factors of oceans such as temperature, oxygen content, and primary productivity affecting on shifting of the distribution of species and ecosystems and indirectly on the phenology and physiology of some marine species, seafood contamination, marine biodiversity and fisheries. However, forecasting the magnitude of such impacts is challenging since responses to climate change are species-specific so that some can adapt to new conditions and others (such as the tropical marine fauna) shift distributions to find more suitable environments and to maintain a physiological optimum.

Our study species including Acanthopagrus latus, Planiliza klunzingeri, and Pomadasys kaakan are high-consumption commercial fishes with high catch in the Persian Gulf. Individually, A. latus as a fish inhabiting warm shallow and coastal waters experiences habitats changes during its life cycle and showed an increasing trend of catch from 2731 to 5410.50 tons in Iranian waters from 2011 to 2013. P. klunzingeri is known as one of the commercially important dominant fish of multispecies fishery with different catch rates between the regions of the Persian Gulf. Finally, the stock assessment of P. kaakan shows its high exploitation rate and the need for decreasing the fishing pressure in the Persian Gulf and Oman Sea. Officially, around 80-85 million tons per year of global marine fisheries landings have been estimated since 1990 with mean annual gross revenues of around USD 100 billion. During the last 60 years, an increasing trend in seafood consumption has been observed, so fish is reported to provide 20 percent of the animal protein needs of 2.9 billion people. Livelihoods of between 660 and 820 million people, directly or indirectly are related to the global fisheries sector. This is especially noticeable in developing countries where people depend on marine resources for food and income and has led to the threat of aquatic resources, such as the Persian Gulf where most of its stocks are under overfishing because of the catching of juvenile species and illegal fishing. It seems that considering the stock status of these commercial fish and the sensitivity to climate change, it is necessary to identify the habitat preferences and to predict distribution changes of these fish reliability under future climate scenarios in the direction of effective conservation of species and to implement sustainable fishing management strategies. Climate change is considered an important challenge that will significantly reorganize the future of global fisheries in the long run and it is accompanied by potential economic impacts. Locally, suggested a high rate of extinction of commercial fish, as well as reduced future catch potential in the Persian Gulf under an 8.5 scenario by 2090. Any small temperature changes can affect marine organisms and related marine capture fisheries. It was expected that climate change would impact maximum catch potential (MCP) through changes in species composition, with predicted increases of MCP in high latitudinal regions and decreases in the tropics.

Habitat suitability (HS) and species distribution modeling (SDM) are suitable tools to predict the habitat preference of one species based on observed relationships of species occurrence records with environmental conditions. These models can predict the possible effects of plausible climate change scenarios on the distribution of marine species through the identification of key habitat variables. The advantages of SDM are the ability to produce long-term, large-scale, and comparable future projections for reliable management and conservation perspectives. MaxEnt as one of the species distribution modeling techniques has attracted a lot of attention in recent years to model the distribution of marine species under future scenarios. MaxEnt modeling algorithms select the best environmental predictor determining species distribution by assigning relative contributions by weighting the variables throughout the analysis. MaxEnt finds the probability distribution of maximum entropy using the environmental covariates at species presence and background points and finally predicts the distribution using environmental variables at species presence. This modeling technique well performs when handling presence-only data and small sample sizes.

Here, we assess the global future distribution of commercial fish A. latus, P.klunzingeri, and P. kaakan under different global warming scenarios (RCP 2.6, RCP 4.5, RCP 6.0, and RCP 8.5) by biogeographic distribution records of species and a set of four environmental variables including mean depth, temperature, salinity, and current velocity (For future period: 2090 to 2100) to predict future distributions and habitat preferences of commercial fish, to understand how environmental variables shape the spatial-temporal patterns of commercial fish, and find out which variable will be more effective in predicting suitable environments of commercial fish under different climate change scenarios. We used different scenarios of climate change and global data of distribution in commercial fish to consider different levels of exposure related to different regions to obtain specific degrees of vulnerability.

## Results
### MaxEnt performance
In Table 1, MaxEnt outputs of the future model under each RCP are provided. Higher iterations express a larger convergence of the model for each species. The future Model showed values of training AUC above 0.99 in all species presenting the high predictive power of MaxEnt to predict the actual distribution of these commercial fishes. Values below the Minimum presence threshold (MPT) show unsuitable habitats for commercial fishes.

### The relative contribution of environmental predictors
The output of the relative contribution of each variable under four RCPs for three species is presented in Fig. 1. These values show the relative importance of environmental predictors including depth, temperature, salinity, and current velocity in predicting the future distribution of the three species under each RCP. In general, the values of relative contribution showed that temperature is the strongest environmental predictors in showing the future distribution of commercial fish under four RCPs. Following temperature, depth was a second dominant predictor for future distribution of fish A. latus and P. kaakan. For P. klunzingeri, salinity was the most important variable after temperature for predicting species distribution under future scenarios. Outputs of Jackknife of AUC for three species also indicated the prominent role of temperature in predicting distribution of commercial fish under future scenarios.

Table 2 shows the outputs of response curves in the MaxEnt model. We observed the significant relationships between environmental variables (Spearman's test; P < 0.05). In the future model, the most suitable habitats were in areas with a depth of 8.12–60.53 m, Temperature of 26.17–31.75°C, salinity of 33.36–40.92 PSU, and currents velocity of 0.001–1.23 m⁻¹. According to Table 2, as the scenario changes from RCP 2.6 towards RCP 8.5, the species would prefer saltier, higher temperatures and deeper waters.

The polynomial curves with five polynomial orders were also plotted to show the non-linear relation between temperature and salinity in latitude 5°. Polynomial curves showed a significant nonlinear relationship between temperature and salinity in P. klunzingeri under all future scenarios. The highest and lowest correlation between temperature and salinity were observed in P. klunzingeri and P. kaakan under RCP 2.6 and 8.5 scenarios, respectively.

### The habitat suitability and environmental variables
Violin plots of Fig. 2a show the range of habitat suitability, temperature, and salinity under future RCPs. The median of habitat suitability was variable from 0.388 (A. latus in RCP 6.0) to 0.529 (P. kaakan in RCP 8.5). Temperature showed significant variations between different RCPs in three species (p = 0.013; Fig. 2a). The minimum (26.14°C) and maximum (29.38°C) of median temperature were observed in A. latus (RCP 2.6) and P. klunzingeri (RCP 8.5). The lowest (33.51 PSU) and highest (34.87 PSU) of salinity was for fish A. latus (RCP 8.5) and P. klunzingeri (RCP 2.6), respectively. The maximum number of distributional records of commercial fish (at latitude 25°–30°N for two species A. latus and P. klunzingeri and 10–15°S for P. kaakan) showed a high correlation with habitat suitability, so the peak of habitat preferences and high probability of occurrence of species were obtained by increasing the number of geographical records. Moreover, the temperature variations of the future scenarios along the latitudes indicated that temperature has a significant role in predicting the occurrence of species, as well as in shaping the pattern observed of geographic records of commercial fish.

### Classification and spatial distribution of habitat suitability
According to the future model of habitat suitability, fish A. latus and P. klunzingeri had a higher percentage of environments with high suitability compared to species P. kaakan. In contrast, fish P. kaakan showed a much higher percentage of environments with medium suitability than the other two species. Under four RCPs, maximum (46.83%) and minimum (2.53%) habitats with high suitability were belonged to fish P. klunzingeri and P. kaakan, respectively. The highest (69.67%) and the lowest (12.87%) percentages of environments with medium suitability were obtained for P. kaakan and P. klunzingeri, respectively. In terms of the percentage of environments with unsuitable conditions, the order of P. klunzingeri (28.07%), A. latus (16.44%) and P. kaakan (9.40%) was observed.

The spatial distribution of habitat suitability showed that for fish A. latus, three areas with high suitability including the northwest of the Persian Gulf, the south of the China Sea, and the west of the Philippine Sea were discernible under the four RCPs. The extent of these areas begins to decrease from RCP 2.6 to RCP 8.5. For P. klunzingeri, the areas with high suitability included the northwest of the Persian Gulf, and around the Strait of Hormuz in the Persian Gulf which showed the increasing trend of the size of high suitable areas towards RCP 8.5. For A. latus, a very low range of environments with high suitability was observed in the Strait of Hormuz and west of the Persian Gulf, and the largest extent of the areas with high suitability belonged to RCP 6.0.

### Observed records and habitat preferences of commercial fish
According to observed distributional records of fishes, species A. latus and P. kaakan showed global distribution in the Persian Gulf, the Oman Sea, and the Indian and Western Pacific Oceans. For P. klunzingeri, records limited to the Persian Gulf, the Oman Sea, the East, and West Indian Ocean, and the Bay of Bengal were visible. According to the RCP scenarios, the habitat preferences of the A. latus in the future would be the Persian Gulf, the South and East China Sea, the Northwest Philippine Sea, and the northern coast of Australia. The Persian Gulf, the Red Sea, and the Lakshadweep tropical archipelago in southern India would be habitat preferences of P. klunzingeri under the future scenarios. Finally for P. kaakan, habitat preferences were included the Persian Gulf and the northern coast of Australia under the future RCPs.

In present model, eco-regions Gulf of Oman, Gulf of Tonkin, and East China Sea are most suitable environments for A. latus. Under climate changes in future, species will prefer environments at higher latitudes including Sea of Japan and Exmouth to Broome (Northwest of Australia). For fish P. klunzingeri, eco-regions with high suitability are Gulf of Oman and Maldives in present model. It seems that species P. klunzingeri will move towards the adjacent areas of eco-regions Gulf of Oman and Maldives including the Persian Gulf and South India and Srilanka following future climate changes. Six eco-regions with high suitability including Gulf of Oman, Western India, Southern China, Papua, Bonaparte coast, and Arnhem coast to Gulf of Carpenteria (The last two eco-regions include the northern coasts of Australia) were recognizable for P. kaakan in present model. Under future scenarios, the distribution of fish P. kaakan would be expand towards higher latitudes and eco-regions South Kuroshio (Adjacent to Southern China) in Northern hemisphere, and Bight of Sofala/Swamp Coast (The eastern coast of Africa in Mozambique), Central and Southern great barrier reef and Ningaloo (Western and Eastern coasts of Australia) in Southern hemisphere would be habitat preferences of this species.

## Discussion
Our findings support that climate changes will probably affect commercial fish through changes in habitat preferences. A gradual poleward distribution expansion was predicted for commercial fish A. latus and P. kaakan across RCP scenarios. We observed a decreasing trend of the environment with high suitability towards the future for P. kaakan (17% in the present compared to 2% in the future model). Our projections suggest variability of the probability of occurrence of the species is higher in habitats of high suitability compared to environments with moderate or low suitability over the scenarios. Changes in the probability of occurrence were strong in regional scales on eco-regions which are probably due to changes in the optimal environmental conditions of commercial fish. These results are consistent with the findings provided by Lima et al., (2022), Silva et al., (2019), and (2016) on pelagic fish which predict a decrease in habitat suitability under future scenarios.

Our future model of all climatic scenarios predicted that potential preference areas of commercial fish are located in depths below 70 m. It was also observed low variability of optima temperature and salinity among scenarios. It seems the environmental optima of commercial fish be species-specific so that habitat suitability will decrease above or below this environmental interactive range. Our projections suggested temperature probably plays the main role in shaping and distributional variability of commercial fish across future scenarios. Many aspects of the organisms' biology and ecology are affected by increased temperature. Local conditions will probably determine the final direction of the consequences of increased temperature on marine organisms. The habitat preferences of our studied species in the present model were mainly subtropical areas. We observed the reduction of suitable habitats in these areas for studied fish under future scenarios. The overview of previous reports indicated that tropical and subtropical zones will be the most affected by increased temperature so that it was observed the negative effects on the physiology of fish, a drop of up to 40% in the capture potential in marine fisheries, reducing landings, and shorter fishing periods. Moreover, the preferred depth of the species can show their sensitivity to climate change. The studied fish may have a higher sensitivity to temperature increase since benthic species have physiologically adapted to constant temperatures under the surface layers and even small temperature changes in the future may have negative effects on these fish.

Following temperature, salinity was the strongest environmental predictor of the distribution of fish P. klunzingeri. Ighab et al., (2019) reported the indirect influence of salinity on sardine distribution while salinity may also be a climate-driven factor inducing shifts in environmental optima. The strong relationship between temperature and salinity in P. klunzingeri indicates the role of salinity on species distribution is probably through its effect on temperature. We observed low variations of current velocity among different scenarios as reported for commercial shrimps and fish and Europen sardine. Moreover, an overview of current velocity and depth showed a higher dependency of three species on deeper and more turbulent waters towards RCP 8.5 by 2100. Abrupt warming can affect stable deeper regions less than superficial waters so that species would probably adapt successfully to the conditions of these regions in the future.

We observed specific responses of commercial fish to ocean warming. Fish A. latus and P. kaakan showed higher sensitivity to climate change with distributional changes towards poles, while P. klunzingeri had moved to nearby habitats. Our study species as highly consumed commercial fish have high regional exploitation rates, especially in the Persian Gulf. resulting in overfishing stocks. Overfishing may aggravate the threats of climate change on stocks of marine species. It is suggested the reduction of fishing intensity on highly suitable habitats for studied commercial fish where fishing hotspots are expected. Moreover, small-scale fisheries may face the greatest impact of global warming (Especially for species with high distribution changes and moving.

## Conclusions
We projected habitat preferences and distribution changes in commercial fish A. latus, P. klunzingeri, and P. kaakan for the first time. The use of a large number of species occurrence records in this study provided high modeling performance in predicting changes in the actual distribution of these commercial fish across future scenarios. Among the four investigated environmental variables, temperature had a significant role in the shaping of the distribution patterns and showing the habitat preferences of these commercial fish. However, the small number of investigated environmental predictors can increase the relative contribution of temperature in predicting the distribution of these commercial fish. The results revealed the sensitivity to climate change is significantly different between the species. Our modeling findings predicted the shrinking of the suitable habitats for these commercial fish, especially in the fish P. kaakan. The findings provided in this study including distribution changes across future scenarios, the percentage of suitable and available habitats, and habitat preferences of these commercial fish can be used as basis data to support and manage these habitats for suitable exploitation of commercial fish stocks. To deal with the inevitable threats of climate change on commercial fish, the precautionary principle suggests human uses and fishing activities should be limited in highly suitable habitats of commercial fish. The use of a wider range of commercial fish and multiple environmental variables, as well as modeling at a regional scale, will help to make a more accurate prediction of the habitat preferences of commercial fish in the future.

## Methods
### Occurrence records of species
Online databases GBIF, OBIS, and literature were used to extract observed records of the geographical distribution of commercial fishes including A. latus, P. klunzingeri, and P. kaakan from Sep to Dec 2022. We extracted the records available in the literature showing fishing landing in the Persian Gulf, Oman Sea, and other sites worldwide. Distribution data of GBIF and OBIS were overlapped to avoid the duplication of records and finally, the total dataset was cleaned (where geographical records were on land, or where records had no geographic coordinates) through ArcMap 10.8.1. Finally, we extracted 1531 geographical records of three species from OBIS (829 records, 54%), GBIF (17 records, 1%), and literature (685 records, 45%).

### Future environmental data
We used the database Bio-ORACLE (Marine data layers for ecological modeling) to extract benthic layers with a minimum depth of environmental variables including temperature (°C), salinity (PSU), and currents velocity (m⁻¹) for a future period (2090–2100) under four RCPs including RCP 2.6, RCP 4.5, RCP 6.0, and RCP 8.5 in the resolution of 5 arc-min. The Global Marine Environmental Datasets (GMED) were used to extract the depth layer at a spatial resolution of 5 arc-min since it was not available in Bio-ORACLE.

### Setting of MaxEnt
MaxEnt 3.4.1e was selected to model the future distribution of commercial fish. We used MaxEnt since it performs well when used as a habitat suitability index, and shows high predictive performance even with small sample sizes. The geographical records of three species (including 371, 171, and 989 records for A. latus, P. klunzingeri, and P. kaakan, respectively) were converted to a single dataset (1531 records) and imported to MaxEnt. Environmental layers of each RCP were separately imported to MaxEnt. The output format of layers in MaxEnt was set to "Logistic" and file type "asc". The importance of environmental predictors was measured through the "jackknife" option. The "Response curve" option was used to assess the relationship between environmental variables and the predicted presence probability of species. The dataset of records was divided into 75% for training and 25% for testing. We configured the maximum number of iterations to 1000 as suggested by Basher and Costello (2016) and Saeedi et al. (2016). The random background points were set at 100,000, and the run type "cross-validate" with 10 replicates was selected. We selected the option "Remove duplicate presence records" to avoid duplicate observations within individual pixels of background environmental layers.

### Output interpretation of MaxEnt
The outputs were separately saved for each RCP. Interpretation of outputs was performed by file "MaxEnt Results". Habitat suitability was interpreted by "logistic model output" (File type: asc). This output shows the presence probability of species, with values defined from 0 to 1, where 0 means no probability of species presence and unsuitable habitat, middle values suggest medium probability of presence and medium suitability of habitat, and 1 indicates the highest probability of presence and high suitability of habitat. The Minimum Presence Threshold (MPT) (Showing the minimum probability of species presence) was used to classify the presence probability of species into four classes including the values below MPT (Not Suitable; NS), MPT to 0.5 (Low Suitability; LS), 0.5–0.75 (Medium Suitability; MS), and 0.75–1 (High Suitability, HS). The performance of MaxEnt was evaluated by the area under the receiver operating characteristic (ROC) curve (AUC) so that values of AUC above 0.9 indicate the great performance of MaxEnt. The relative importance of environmental variables in predicting the future distribution of commercial fish was assessed through outputs "the percent variable contribution" and "jack-knife" in MaxEnt.

---

# Pesticide budgets in land and rivers

## Abstract
Pesticides are ubiquitous environmental pollutants negatively affecting ecosystem and human health. About 3 Tg of pesticides are used annually in agriculture to protect crops. How much of these pesticides remain on land and reach the aquifer or the ocean is uncertain. Monitoring their environmental fate is challenging, and a detailed picture of their mobility in time and space is largely missing. Here, we develop a process-based model accounting for the hydrology and biogeochemistry of the 92 most used agricultural pesticide active substances to assess their pathways through the principal catchments of the world and draw a near-present picture of the global land and river budgets, including discharge to oceans. Of the 0.94 Tg net annual pesticide input in 2015 used in this study, 82% is biologically degraded, 10% remains as residue in soil and 7.2% leaches below the root zone. Rivers receive 0.73 Gg of pesticides from their drainage at a rate of 10 to more than 100 kg yr⁻¹ km⁻¹. By contrast to their fate in soil, 1.1% of pesticides entering rivers are degraded along streams, exceeding safety levels (concentrations >1 μg l⁻¹) in more than 13,000 km of river length, with 0.71 Gg of pesticide active ingredients released to oceans every year. Herbicides represent the prevalent pesticide residue on both land (72%) and river outlets (62%).

## Main
River catchments play an extraordinary intermediary role in terrestrial ecosystems. They integrate all environmental processes, both natural and anthropogenic, within their drainage basins and export their cumulative impacts to catchment endpoints, be they rivers, wetlands, lakes, estuaries or oceans. Among anthropogenic processes, agriculture has the most extensive spatial footprint, using 48 million km² of land globally (50% of habitable land). Relevant river catchments reflect irrigation, impoundments, water diversions and abstractions in those areas, with water quality greatly affected by agrochemicals. Inputs of nitrogen (N) and phosphorus (P) used in fertilizers for agriculture — 105 Tg N yr⁻¹ and 43 Tg P yr⁻¹ in 2019 — are well-recognized sources of water pollution with 32–45% of N and 10–20% of P exported to oceans via rivers. By contrast, pesticide inputs and their environmental fate have received much less attention. This is possibly because the annual global input, about 3 Tg yr⁻¹ of pesticide active substances (PAS), is a small fraction of N and P inputs, having seldom visible direct effects on water and soil pollution. In practice, they are more difficult to measure. However, PAS are engineered toxicants whose pollution impacts may be as important as, or more important than, fertilizers. There is evidence that non-negligible amounts of pesticide residues remain in soils and surface waters, with great potential to harm a wide spectrum of non-target organisms in exposed ecosystems. The few studies available today lack critical information, focusing only on aggregated pesticide classes. Conversely, the fate of individual PAS via rivers and their discharge into oceans — needed to assess ecosystem damage and health risks — remains unexplored.

The biogeochemistry of pesticides is complex. Pesticides can be catabolized by soil microbes, undergo transport in deep soil strata and dilution in aquifers and surface waters. Interactions with mineral surfaces reduce further breakdown by enzymes or other agents such as ultraviolet radiation and heat. The concentrations observed in river waters generally range from below 1 μg up to several milligrams per litre and are associated with enormous toxicity loads. Many PAS have regulatory threshold limits (RTLs) much smaller than 1 μg l⁻¹ for fish, aquatic invertebrates and aquatic plants. At the same time, bioaccumulation through successive trophic levels of the food chain can amplify concentrations in aquatic lifeforms by 1,000-fold or more. Despite current understanding of the important threat of pesticides to biological systems, and hence human health, there is no standard approach in the prognosis of pesticide presence in the environment and specifically in rivers. Existing pesticide-specific models are suited for field and catchment scales (for example, GLEAMS, MACRO, RZWQM2, PEARL, PRZM, Pelmo, LEACHM/LEACHP, HYDRUS-1D, ZIM-AgriTra and FRM), whereas others are used to study selected pesticides at catchment scales (for example, CATHY, SWAT and DynAPlus) and global scales (BRTSim). Whereas current models can predict riverine concentrations of some PAS in small or intermediate size catchments sufficiently well over time scales of hours to weeks, they are known to perform poorly over seasonal time scales.

Here, we address this gap by conducting a global budget of individual PAS, from their application points within the main agricultural catchments downstream to rivers and oceans. The assessment gives a near-present picture of pesticide fate, identifying pollution hotspots in which more accurate monitoring could lead to improved risk mitigation actions. We used the BRTSim v.5.0b model, newly parameterized with soil physical and hydraulic properties, land cover and land use characteristics and hydrometeorological variables, to study the fate of the 92 PAS most relevant in agriculture, with reference to the year 2015. We considered the 144 principal catchments of the world using a georeferenced grid at 0.25° resolution (about 30 km at the equator). The net annual pesticide inputs on the total catchment area was 0.94 Tg in 2015.

### The land budget
Of the 0.94 Tg pesticide annual net inputs, losses from the root zone (RZ; 0–100 cm) corresponded to 90% ± 15%, of which 82% ± 15% was biodegraded, 7.2% ± 1.3% leached below the soil RZ, less than 0.1% converged into river reaches and a negligible part was released as direct runoff into oceans outside rivers (the range represents the 68% confidence interval (CI) across all PAS for these and all subsequent figures). At near-steady state, the residue in the RZ was highly uncertain, representing 0–28% of the net inputs, with a highly variable 0–23% fraction immobilized by adsorption to soil organic matter (SOM), 3% ± 2% residing in aqueous solution in soil, 0.2% volatilized and less than 0.01% precipitated. Important geographic variability characterized specific hydrological basins, with the adsorbed fraction generally higher in catchments rich in soil organic carbon at high and low latitudes and with a higher leaching fraction in catchments experiencing high rainfall rates within the tropics. The estimated leaching to the deep soil of up to about 7.2% of net inputs (67.8 Gg annually) was consistent with 4.3–7.3% and between 0.1% and 5% from field measurements of selected herbicides and insecticides. The potential long-term aquifer contamination by many PAS is supported by evidence that European aquifers continue to be contaminated by atrazine today even after the European Union (EU) banned its use in 2004.

The estimated 3.2% PAS residue in aqueous solution in the RZ (28.7 Gg) consisted of 72.5% herbicides (with primary PAS being glyphosate), 16.6% multipurpose substances (used for more than one group of target organisms, mostly metam potassium), 9.9% fungicides (mainly mancozeb) and 1.1% insecticides (mainly chlorpyrifos). This residue in the near-surface soil is of great relevance: first, it consists of undegraded PAS that retain their design toxicity and can become adverse agents for non-target organisms; second, it is prone to mobilization within the environment, representing the main component of the land yield into streams and rivers. The 7% average adsorbed residue (68.1 Gg) mostly consisted of herbicides, fungicides and insecticides with a prevalence of PAS similar to the aqueous residue but with negligible presence of multipurpose PAS. These PAS are mostly represented by metam and metam potassium, which have low affinity for adsorption to SOM and are therefore highly mobile in soil.

### The river budget
The pesticide yield from application points within catchments into rivers was calculated as the transport of undegraded mobile PAS in the soil aqueous phase throughout the catchment via direct runoff and overland flow until convergence with main rivers. The average annual pesticide overland flow was about 7.7% of the net inputs by mass and was spatially variable, with hotspots corresponding to agricultural areas with high PAS application rates and subject to high rainfall rates. At near-steady state, less than 0.1% (0.73 Gg) of the annual net inputs to catchments entered rivers, with about 13,400 km receiving between 10 and 100 kg yr⁻¹ km⁻¹ and about 1,400 km receiving more than 100 kg yr⁻¹ km⁻¹. Rivers mostly exposed to pesticide land yield are located in central and western United States (Mississippi and Sacramento), Argentina (Parana), India (Ganges), East China (Yangtze, Pearl and Yellow (Huang He) River) and South East Asia (Irrawaddy and lower Mekong). Rivers in Europe received an intermediate pesticide yield from land with hotspots along the Po and Danube, whereas rivers in Africa and Oceania received less inputs from land with the exception of the lower Niger and reaches of the Congo.

Within the rivers considered, 2.6% ± 0.9% of the pesticide land yield resided in river waters whereas 97.4% ± 1.3% loss included 1.1% ± 0.4% removal by biodegradation and 96.3% ± 0.9% (about 0.71 Gg annually) export all the way along rivers to oceans. The composition of pesticide mixtures in river waters was 52.6% herbicides, 35.6% multipurpose PAS, 11.2% fungicides and 0.6% insecticides and is analysed in greater detail in the next section. The discharge to oceans consists of 62.9% herbicides, 26.8% multipurpose PAS, 9.7% fungicides and 0.7% insecticides. In these classes, glyphosate, metam potassium, chlorothalonil and chlorpyrifos, respectively, were the most prevalent PAS. An extensive analysis of pesticide mobility in the upper Mississippi catchments showed that the load of the streams was no more than 2% of applied mass, in line with our finding.

### Concentrations in rivers and discharge to oceans
Pesticides entering rivers and travelling downstream undergo dilution depending on streamflow regime and biodegradation processes. We explicitly accounted for pesticide inputs into rivers, variable stream discharge and water stage within river reaches and biodegradation to assess the temporal concentration of individual PAS in rivers. We found that 62,100 km of rivers had average cumulative pesticide concentrations below detection limits of 10⁻³ μg l⁻¹, 140,300 km had concentrations up to 1 μg l⁻¹ and more than 13,000 km above 1 μg l⁻¹. We compared the simulated long-term average concentration of individual PAS against existing independent observations from national authorities in 15 locations in the United States, 2 in Canada, 57 in the EU and 2 in Australia. Within the high variability in the data considered, our estimates generally underestimated observations. In-stream continuous measurements of PAS concentrations show daily variability of one to two orders of magnitude, mainly due to the intermittency of loads, complexity of biogeochemical degradation and interactions with sediment deposition and resuspension — not necessarily associated with the size of the stream or catchment area. A comparison of our estimates against country-averaged PAS concentrations in rivers in Argentina, Brazil, China, Egypt, India and Pakistan further indicated modelling underestimation of observed PAS concentrations in rivers. Hence, the actual threat to resident non-target organisms from pesticide use and transport in rivers may be larger than estimated here. This underestimation may be related to pesticide inputs lower than actual and/or lower yield to rivers, the last resulting from overestimated biodegradation rates, leaching to deep soil or soil immobilization by adsorption. Consequently, the overall export to ocean may be greater than 0.71 Gg estimated here.

To provide a wider context to our findings, we calculated the frequency of occurrence of river concentrations in excess to RTLs established for aquatic invertebrates, aquatic plants and fish. We found that measured PAS concentrations in some stations of the United States and the EU were in excess of RTLs. Further model analysis showed that several river reaches in North America and East Asia and to a lesser extent in Europe, exceeded one or more RTL at least once a year.

In our work, the greatest exporters to oceans by continent were the Mississippi (North America), the Amazon, Parana, Uruguay, Araguaya and Magdalena (South America), the Po (Europe), the Niger and Congo (Africa) and the Ganges, Irrawaddy, Mekong, Pearl, Yangtze, Yellow, Liao and Amur rivers (Asia), each exceeding 5,000 kg of PAS every year.

### Study implications
The global budget of selected PAS helps to answer two fundamental questions. Where do pesticides go after release in the environment? How much of each PAS remains on land and how much reaches the oceans? Using a full-scale spatially and temporally explicit process-based dynamic model as a diagnostic tool, we investigated pesticide fluxes through the environment from their point sources, producing a picture of mobility and accumulation of undegraded PAS. We informed our model with the most up-to-date geospatially detailed data on pesticide use in ten cropping systems globally.

We identify three key findings from this assessment. First, we estimate that more than 80% of the net 0.94 Tg annual inputs of PAS are degraded biologically in soil — highlighting the importance of soil health — leaving about 0.16 Tg of undegraded PAS in the environment. Second, about 67.6 Gg of PAS, nearly half of undegraded PAS, leaches below the RZ where biological activity capable of breakdown of PAS is substantially lower than near the surface. Leached PAS are therefore prone to accumulation in aquifers leading to potential groundwater pollution. Third, about 0.71 Gg are discharged annually to oceans. Although this amount is less than 0.1% of net inputs, it is a cause of decreased species richness of stream invertebrates with little known consequences on near-coast ecosystems. Furthermore, we note that our estimates of biodegradation in soils may be underestimating pollution risk, as they assume direct PAS mineralization to end products, whereas in many observed cases PAS may degrade into a cascade of daughter substances which can be as toxic as the parent and occasionally even more persistent.

Identifying good proxies for predicting pesticide movement and accumulation points is a difficult task. We conducted a statistical analysis of our modelling results to identify which variable exerted a prevalent control on soil residue and river discharge to ocean. Our analyses suggest that soil residues were prevalently controlled by, in decreasing order: PAS application rate; mean annual soil water saturation and temperature; and soil organic carbon content. The variables mostly controlling the discharge to oceans are, in decreasing order: application rate; crop surface area; watershed surface area; and river length. Although the PAS input is tightly correlated to residues and discharges because it is the actual source of pesticides in the environment, the weak effects on discharges to oceans of mean annual precipitation, temperature and soil organic carbon are less intuitive. These findings, in particular the key role played by PAS application rates, confirm the importance of continuing international efforts aimed at achieving a more sustainable and productive agriculture.

## Methods
### Data source
The datasets used to design the computational domain and conduct our modelling are detailed in Supplementary Table 2.

### Conceptual framework
The assessment was conducted in two steps: first, we determined the pesticide budget within catchments and their yield into rivers; next, we determined river budgets and export to oceans. At the catchment surface, the model calculates water direct runoff generation and overland routing, including pesticides transport where these are used. In the subsurface, the model accounts for the biogeochemistry and transport of PAS through soil. Within river reaches, the model accounts for pesticide inputs from upstream and overland flow, biochemistry and downstream transport including discharge to oceans.

### Computational domain and catchment selection
The global spatial domain (180° W, 180° E and 90° S, 90° N) was described at a resolution of 0.25° (about 30 km at the equator). We selected all hydrological catchments corresponding to rivers with an accumulation greater than 5,000 cells upstream in HydroRIVERS v.1.0 at its original 500 m resolution. We excluded all rivers not discharging into oceans or represented by fewer than five grid cells. We also excluded catchments with an agricultural area smaller than 20% of the catchment area based on available crop maps. In the 144 catchments selected, we accounted for up to the tenth most distant grid cells (with and without agriculture) uphill from both river sides limitedly to the catchment divide. Grid cells further away contributed little to overland water and PAS flows. This leads to about 64,000 grid cells comprising 16.7 million km² of agricultural land within a total catchment area of 41.7 million km². Each grid cell includes 1 m of atmosphere and extends in soil from the land surface to 56.5 m depth with vertical discretization and hydraulics from SOIL-WATERGRIDS. Rivers occupied nearly 7,500 grid cells or about 211,600 km.

We parameterized the vertical soil profiles with sand, silt and clay mass fractions, bulk density and porosity from SoilGrids, water residual saturation and permeability, air-entry suction and pore volume distribution index values, heat conductivity and capacity, adjusted for depth dependence and for equilibrium between volumetric water volume (θ) and depth of water table (WTD). Plant roots were distributed exponentially from surface to 1 m depth with land cover-specific average root depth. The initial θ values were assigned the average of data in the NOAH/GLDAS v.2, satellite data from the European Space Agency, Climate Change Initiative for Soil Moisture (ESA/CCI), data-assimilation soil moisture from the Global Land Evaporation Amsterdam Model (GLEAM) and the ground-based soil moisture measurements from the International Soil Moisture Network (ISMN). WTD was initialized with the long-term average. Temperature was initialized with data from NOAA/NCEI. Finally we initialized the pH and organic carbon mass fraction in soil using SoilGrids.

We described river reach in the ith grid cell as a finite element with variable volume with width sourced from ref. 51, length approximated as LR = (LG + LG 2)/2, with LG the size of the hosting grid cells and depth centred about the average provided in ref. 51 but variable on a daily time scale depending on the river flow discharge provided by the Global Food Awareness System (GloFAS).

### Pesticide selection
We sourced annual application rates of 95 PAS from PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1. Application rates were estimated by re-analysis of the USGS Pesticide National Synthesis Project database in the United States, which reports 512 PAS in six main crops (alfalfa, corn, cotton, rice, soybean and wheat) and four aggregated crops (vegetables and fruit, orchards and grapes, pasture and hay and other crops) from 1992 to 2016. The top 20 PAS most used by mass in each crop were selected, yielding a total of 95 unique PAS. Estimation of PAS application rates at global scale was then conducted by spatial statistical methods accounting for 20 covariates. Next, national factors (for example, policy, agricultural practices, machinery capacity and access) were accounted for by constraining estimates against country-level pesticide-use data reported in FAOSTAT and by country-specific approvals for adopting genetically modified pesticide-resistant crops of the International Service for the Acquisition of Agri-Biotech Applications, as well as country-specific pesticide bans. The pesticide application rates in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 were not constrained against FAO in 28 countries because of unavailable data at the time of simulation. The estimates were next benchmarked at various levels including: validation of spatial inference methods; conditioning against pesticide classes in FAOSTAT; independent national datasets available before 2019 (United Kingdom and Australia); literature data for South Korea and South Africa; and manufacturer recommendations for selected PAS estimated at particularly high rates (metam potassium, chloropicrin, dichloropropene, chlorothalonil and calcium polysulfide). The estimated global annual applied PAS mass of 3.6 Tg in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 was based on, and thus fully consistent with, FAOSTAT application rate data available at the time of this work in 2019. A substantial downward revision of FAOSTAT figures, specifically for China, made in 2022–2023 after the completion of this assessment, could not be considered. Once included in future simulations, we expect that our estimates of river discharge to oceans along the Chinese coast will likewise be revised downwards.

For this work, we excluded Bacillus amyloliquefaciens, calcium polysulfide and petroleum oil from the original 95 PAS in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 because of insufficient knowledge of their physicochemical properties and ecotoxicities. These 92 PAS comprise 55 herbicides, 17 insecticides, 16 fungicides and 4 multipurpose PAS; that is, some fumigants with many target organisms. Because PAS are specific to ten different cropping systems, we first aggregated median annual application rates and then determined daily rates according to crop calendars and growing season using previous methods. From daily rates, we detracted 40% to account for losses by leaves interception and long-range wind drift, resulting in 0.94 Tg yr⁻¹ of total net annual inputs of the 92 PAS in the selected catchments. PAS used but banned before 2015 were not accounted for even if potentially still present in the environment in 2015.

### Hydrological modelling
We used two approaches to account for catchment and river hydrology. For catchment hydrology (surface and belowground) we adopted the framework of SOIL-WATERGRIDS. In brief, water sources and sinks in the ith grid cell of interest include global daily precipitation Pi of the Climatic Research Unit gridded Time Series dataset (CRU/TS), daily actual evapotranspiration ETAi from the GLEAM, and monthly snowmelt SMi from Global Land Data Assimilation System (NOAH/GLDAS) from 1986 to 2015. In the same year range, we calculated irrigation IRRi as a function of the precipitation and actual evapotranspiration gap and the direct runoff generation RNFi,out using the curve number method. Overland flow routing of RNFi,out to the kth grid cell accounted for the steepest down-slope gradient calculated using a digital elevation map. The hydrological model produced the time- and depth-resolved volumetric soil water content θi and WTD, necessary to assess the biogeochemistry and transport of selected PAS within catchments and, in particular, determine the fraction fi entering rivers. Within the RZ of the ith grid cell, the water balance was Δθi = (Pi − ETAi + SMi + IRRi + (RNFi∥in − RNFi∥out) − Li)Δt, where RNFi∥in accumulates the incoming overland runoff from many directions and Li is the water leaching below or uplift from the RZ, solved using the Richards equation.

For the river hydrology, the time-resolved water volume change in the river reach of the ith grid cell was calculated as ΔVi = (Qi − Qavg,i)Δt, where Qi and Qavg,i are daily and long-term mean river flow discharges, calculated using daily stream velocity from GloFAS and river geometry in ref. 51.

### Biogeochemical modelling
The dynamics of the 92 selected PAS were described explicitly in space and time and accounted for the advection and diffusion in surface, subsurface and streams and for biodegradation, mineral interaction and solubility specific to each PAS. Here, using net inputs (Ai,j) of the jth PAS in the ith grid cell, we calculated the time and depth-resolved aqueous mass (Wi,j) net overland runoff RNFi,j = (1 − fi)RNFi,j∥in − RNFi,j∥out where fi is the river surface area fraction in the ith grid cell (described above) determining the fraction of PAS entering rivers from overland runoff and with RNFi,j∥out = RNFi × Ci,j where Ci,j is the aqueous concentration of the jth PAS in top soil (0–30 cm) of the ith grid cell. We also accounted for PAS gas exsolution (Gi,j), linear sorption to SOM (Si,j) and precipitation (Pi,j) using the mass action law at local equilibrium as largely detailed in ref. 26, with physicochemical parameters for PAS listed in Supplementary Table 1. Finally, we accounted for biodegradation (Bi,j) using composite pseudo first-order kinetics of the form ΔCi,j = fi,j × k × Ci,j × SOCiΔt, where fi,j is soil microbial activity response to environmental temperature, soil water saturation and pH, k = −ln(0.5)/t½ is biodegradation rate constant calculated from the field half-life t½ and SOC is the cometabolic organic carbon content from ref. 40. These allowed us to determine the residue Ri,j = (Wi + RNFi + Gi + Si + Pi) in the RZ (0–100 cm depth) and losses Di,j = (Bi + RNFi + Li) for each jth PAS, where RNFi,j is direct overland runoff RNFi,j∥out to oceans outside rivers and Li,j = Li × Ci,j is the leaching below RZ. The mass balance at the catchment prescribes ΔRi,j = (A − D)i,jΔt for individual PAS.

In river reaches, inputs from overland runoff in the ith grid cell were calculated for the jth PAS as Ii,j = fiRNFi,j∥in and from the kth upstream reach and tributaries as Fi,j∥in = Qi × Cᵣi,j with Cᵣi,j the concentration of the jth PAS in the incoming river water. Discharge to downstream reaches or the ocean was calculated as advective flow, Fi,j∥out = Qi × Cᵣi,j. The time-resolved aqueous mass residue in river water was calculated as Wᵣi,j = Vi × Cᵣi,j. Finally, we accounted for biodegradation in rivers (Bᵣi,j) similarly as in soil (using the same field half-life) but using the average dissolved organic carbon concentration in rivers from ref. 67 instead of SOC. The mass balance at river reaches prescribes ΔWᵣi,j = (I + Fin − Fout − Bᵣ)i,jΔt for individual PAS.

### Computational shell
The BRTSim v.5.0b computational environment was used in this work.

### Validation
The hydrological modelling framework SOIL-WATERGRIDS was previously validated against independent data of volumetric soil water content, depth of water table, land ponding (wetlands) and overland runoff. Here, we report on the long-term average anomaly, correlation and seasonality across all simulated years in three quantities determining PAS biogeochemistry, namely: (1) soil water saturation against averages from ESA/CCI, GLEAM and GLDAS datasets showed a slight bias and a non-negligible dispersion, compared to validation datasets based on the volumetric soil water content in ref. 39; (2) land surface temperature matched relatively well, with some dispersion against data in NOAA; and (3) soil average pH matched well, with low data dispersion against SoilGrids2.

There is presently no public access to georeferenced soil residue of individual PAS and country-aggregation of field sampling available in ref. 12 for the EU were considered to have too low data density to be statistically reliable for comparison. However, we validated the estimated near-steady state (simulated year 25 to 30) long-term mean concentration of PAS in rivers, with observations in 15 locations in the United States, 2 in Canada, 57 in the EU and 2 locations in Australia, with concentrations in each location representing averages from one or many stations from 2010 to 2016.

Finally, we collected data of PAS concentrations in rivers in Argentina, Brazil, China, Egypt, India and Pakistan from field measurements, review and meta-analysis publications for comparison with country-averaged river concentrations of individual PAS in our assessment.

### Time frame for analyses and steady state
Our assessment refers to PAS application rates of year 2015. Dynamic modelling required 30 years with hydrometeorological forcing from 1986 to 2015 to approach a near-steady state in the hydrological and biogeochemical processes of interest. Near-steady state was detected if rates of change of the PAS partitioning in the environmental compartments were less than 5% starting from no pesticide at the initialization time. We found that this criterion was satisfied from year 25. All quantities reported by long-term averages refer to the 30-year simulation unless otherwise detailed.

### Budget variability to inputs
We could not conduct a stochastic uncertainty analysis at full scale because of the many parameters describing PAS and environmental parameters in the model throughout the computational domain. However, we repeated the same temporally and spatially explicit modelling and land budget in the catchments of North America using the high and low estimates in PAS application rates in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 and analysed the corresponding variability in residues and losses, as reported in Extended Data Table 1.

### Budget variability across PAS
With access to the land and river budget of individual PAS, we estimated the ranges of variability in all contributions to the residues and losses of Fig. 1a,b by calculating the standard deviation (68% CI) from data reported in the Supplementary Data.

### Principal and confounding effects
We analysed average PAS residue in the RZ and average annual discharge to oceans by hydrological basin against several covariates using a partial least square (PLS) fitting combined with principal component analysis to identify the best proxies to residues and discharges. The covariates included: PAS total annual applied mass, catchment and crop surface areas, mean annual temperature, precipitation, overland flow and soil saturation of the catchment, mean soil organic carbon in the catchment RZ, maximum elevation point, total river length within the catchment. After variables were normalized by their standard deviations, we determined percentage variance in residues and discharges associated to the covariates (plsregress function in Matlab2021a) and the covariate importance using PLS normalized weights and the squares of the PLS scores. Covariates were sorted by decreasing percentage variance and covariate importance.

### Study limitations
Several limitations apply to this work. Although the selected 92 PAS in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 were the most relevant in agriculture — as they include the 20 most used substances in the 10 most globally relevant crops, representing 84% of applied mass globally after conditioning to the FAOSTAT data for the year 2015 — they only partially represented global pesticide use. On the one hand, we excluded the following: agricultural PAS banned before 2015 and used in substantially small amounts; PAS used on livestock and in aquaculture because of a lack of reported data; and PAS used in public urban spaces and private dwellings, for which no global statistics nor geospatial data exist at present. On the other hand, we did not explicitly account for subsistence agriculture, local cropping systems and traditional cultivations for which use of chemical pesticides can be much smaller. We thus expect that our work generally underestimates the masses in the global land and river budgets. By contrast, pesticide land and river budgets in the catchments in the Democratic Republic of the Congo, Gabon, Nigeria and Somalia may suffer from either overestimation or underestimation because PAS application rates in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 were not constrained against FAO for lack of FAOSTAT data before 2019 at the time of our simulations. The relative values in the land budget may suffer from uncertainty in the biogeochemical modelling of pesticides because of parametric uncertainty in adsorption, solubility, gas exsolution and biodegradation. We did not explicitly include intermediate metabolic products because of limited knowledge of all kinetics and decomposition pathways associated with each PAS and for the complexity of potential PAS interactions, still largely unknown. The relative values in the river budget may underestimate the discharge to ocean because we did not explicitly include soil erosion as a contributor to PAS mobility throughout land and via sediment transport in rivers. Although we tested the variability in PAS application rates reported in PEST-CHEMGRIDS v.1 and we benchmarked our estimates in the global river budget against observed river concentrations in the United States, Canada, the EU, Australia, and in Argentina, Brazil, China, Egypt, India and Pakistan, other sources of uncertainty not quantified herein may affect our estimates. These include: model structure uncertainty associated to the pseudo three-dimensional modelling of land and soil, potentially affecting overland and subsurface lateral transport of PAS; short-range spray drift and direct deposition into surface water of strongly sorbing PAS; long-range atmospheric drift and deposition of PAS, excluded for a lack of modelling capability in this work; and uncertainty propagation in the harmonization of environmental datasets used to feed BRTSim v.5.0b at the working resolution. Although with limitations, this assessment helps to quantify the potential environmental risk of chemical pesticides globally, with geospatial detail.
