Likewise, driven by the Ganges, the riverine Hg input rate in the Bay of Bengal is 26-fold higher than the atmospheric Hg deposition.

The South Atlantic Ocean receives the largest flux of riverine Hg (319 Mg yr⁻¹ with a range of 231–437 Mg yr⁻¹) followed by the Indian Ocean, primarily driven by the Amazon and Ganges rivers, respectively. Although large amounts of suspended sediment from the Amazon River are deposited within the river plume in the Southern Hemisphere, there is the potential for long range transport of Amazon River water across the Equator. On the continental scale, Asian rivers are of particular concern as more than half of the top 100 contributors are located there, and five of these rivers (the Ganges, Yangtze, Godavari, Irrawaddy and Xi rivers) contribute half of the flux from Asia. These rivers all flow through some of the most heavily populated areas in Asia. Rivers discharging from low latitudes (30°N–30°S) contribute 813 and 7.6 Mg yr⁻¹ which is approximately 80% of the global flux for both Hg and MeHg, respectively. By contrast, 36 ± 7.0 Mg yr⁻¹ of Hg is exported from the rivers in the pan-Arctic region. Our estimate for the pan-Arctic region is within the range reported by previous studies (that is, 8.0–108 Mg yr⁻¹) and is quite close to recent estimates by Sonke et al. and Zolkos et al., which were extrapolated from observations in major Arctic rivers (that is, 44 ± 4 and 37 ± 1.5 Mg yr⁻¹, respectively). Although three of the ten largest rivers in terms of basin area and length in the world are located in the Arctic (that is, the Yenisei, Ob and Lena), their Hg concentrations (average of 10 ng l⁻¹ according to our model) are much lower than those in the mid and low latitudes of the Northern Hemisphere (19 and 37 ng l⁻¹, respectively), due to less severe soil erosion and a lack of primary Hg sources. As geogenic Hg only amounts to 0–40% of all Hg in Arctic soils, the flux from Arctic rivers probably represents a legacy source of Hg that was emitted from lower latitudes and deposited in the Arctic.

As with other biogeochemical cycles, the Amazon River and other large river basins are important to global fluxes; however, our results identify hot spots in basins of a variety of sizes. Yields of riverine Hg export (flux divided by drainage area) are largest for Java Island (Asia, 113 μg m⁻² yr⁻¹), followed by Taiwan Island (Asia, 67 μg m⁻² yr⁻¹), the Ganges-Brahmaputra River Basin (Asia, 66 μg m⁻² yr⁻¹) and the Virgin Islands (North America, 64 μg m⁻² yr⁻¹), as well as other small island river systems in the Greater Antilles (North America). These yields are greater than the global average by factors of 9–15, primarily due to high rainfall density and high mountainous slopes that lead to increased erosion and/or high levels of human activities. For instance, the high yield in the Ganges-Brahmaputra River Basin is related to intensive coal combustion in India and erosion in the Himalaya mountain system. River basins in Indonesia are important contributors to Asian Hg exports. For instance, 7 of the top 20 Asian river basins in terms of the yield are located in Indonesia, primarily due to high densities of rainfall and litterfall, which drive Hg to tropical soils. Additionally, the high yield of riverine Hg export in Indonesia may partly reflect the impact of the intentional use of Hg to extract gold from gold-bearing soils and rocks in artisanal and small-scale gold mining in this country, although the impact of artisanal and small-scale gold mining on the environment remains largely uncertain.

Our model captures the temporal variability of river Hg export resulting from flood events in wet seasons, which have not been represented by other global estimates previously. For instance, the export in the pan-Arctic reaches 11 Mg month⁻¹ during the spring flood, one order of magnitude greater than that during dry seasons. Likewise, wet seasons are responsible for a large proportion of the annual Hg exports of many large rivers in lower latitudes, such as the Amazon and Ganges (up to eight- and nine-fold larger than that during dry seasons, respectively), reflecting a substantially increased mobility of previously deposited legacy anthropogenic Hg in soil induced by hydrologic events. Recent studies concluded that Hg from river floods drove a net export of marine Hg⁰ back to the atmosphere, highlighting the impact of hydrologic events on the regional Hg cycle. We suggest that hydrologic events such as monsoonal rainfall, which are largely influenced by climate change, could trigger large exports of terrestrial Hg into coastal oceans due to larger river discharge with stronger erosive power and subsequently intensify regional and seasonal Hg cycles.

The uncertainties associated with global Hg and MeHg exports are 8.3% and 8.0% (standard deviation), respectively. Considering different oceans, the uncertainties in Hg are highest in rivers flowing into inland seas (that is, the Black and Caspian seas, 142%) and lowest in the Mediterranean Sea (8.1%). Our model results correlate well with observational data, such as the Yangtze (47 versus 48 Mg yr⁻¹ results of our model and observations, respectively), Yenisei (3.6 versus 3.6–5.2 Mg yr⁻¹) and St. Lawrence (1.3 versus 1.2 Mg yr⁻¹) rivers. The estimated flux for the Amazon River is 32% lower than the observation, presumably due to the higher fractions of dissolved Hg (~50%) that have been measured there. It is hard to accurately reproduce Hg fluxes for small rivers owing to the larger variations in the fluxes for these rivers induced by hydrologic events. In addition, our estimation might be conservative since the reproduction of Hg fluxes of some highly polluted rivers caused by large amounts of wastewater discharge is a big challenge, and further observation is required to calibrate the model.

## Impacts of the river mercury export on the mercury cycle
Previous studies have hypothesized that river Hg export is important for specific coastal areas. Our findings draw further attention to the role of rivers and enhance our understanding of the function of rivers in the global Hg cycle. Here, we construct a new, empirically constrained mechanistic model to quantitatively demonstrate that rivers are the largest source of Hg to the global coastal ocean (45%, while atmospheric deposition and open ocean contributed the rest). Furthermore, our analysis suggests that river Hg exports substantially impact the global Hg cycle. First, our modelling exercise demonstrates a larger amount of coastal Hg sedimentation potentially due to our choice of partition coefficient for Hg, which leads to a greater amount of open-ocean Hg entering coastal waters sorbing to particles that are deposited in coastal sediment. Interestingly, despite this large input and removal of open-ocean Hg in coastal waters, our model predicts that global coastal oceans are a net source of Hg to the open ocean, which is driven by our new, larger riverine Hg flux. That is, if we use the lower riverine flux from the UNEP (that is, 300 Mg yr⁻¹), we predict the net coastal to oceanic Hg flux to be near zero compared with a large coastal to ocean flux using the new riverine flux of 1,000 Mg yr⁻¹ estimated here. Another implication of the larger riverine flux is a smaller ocean to atmosphere exchange. Additionally, the larger riverine input increases the estimate of the global Hg burial in continental shelf regions to 730 (410–1,100, interquartile range from the Monte Carlo simulation) Mg yr⁻¹, which is greater than both the UNEP estimate (200 Mg yr⁻¹) and our estimate using a smaller riverine flux. Thus, we argue that continental shelves may have a higher Hg burden than previously thought. There is still high uncertainty around these estimates because current Earth system models lack representation of estuarine/coastal processes. Given that coastal oceans contribute substantially to human exposure to MeHg, we suggest that future global Hg models may need to refine the process of Hg transport along the river-ocean continuum.

Hg cycling within coastal waters is highly sensitive to climate-induced changes and anthropogenic disturbances. Higher river flows with stronger erosive power are responsible for larger riverine Hg and nutrient fluxes, while climate change has been projected to induce an increase in river discharge for many coastal regions globally. Other climate-related threats, such as thawing permafrost, may also alter terrestrial hydrology and soil biogeochemistry, and could mobilize legacy Hg that is stored in soils and fluvial sediments. Agricultural and other activities accelerate soil erosion and result in larger river sediment fluxes, while humans reduce fluxes of sediment to rivers downstream due to retention within reservoirs. However, increasing releases of Hg from gold mining and fossil fuel combustion may offset the decline in the river export due to river damming. Furthermore, the resuspension of buried riverine Hg on the continental shelves due to intensive human activities (for example, sediment mining and aquaculture) might also provide a substrate for water column methylation. Our results, together with recent regional observations on river Hg dynamics, provide a consistent perspective that the riverine Hg export is disproportionately important to the global Hg cycle.

## Methods
We constructed an observation-based database of 32 estimates of river Hg and MeHg exports from seven continents to nine oceans/seas, including six spatially explicit estimates. This database was based on the collection and calculation of field observations from published peer-reviewed literature and includes measurements of different Hg species and ancillary parameters, such as flow rate, suspended sediment flux and concentration and DOC concentration following our previous strategy. River Hg measurements were generally available after the year 2000. The majority of the measurements in the included studies were conducted using cold vapour atomic fluorescence spectrometry or cold vapour atomic absorption spectrometry for Hg concentration and gas chromatography cold vapour atomic fluorescence spectrometry for MeHg concentration. Hg data from infrequently used methods were not included in the dataset. Hg concentration measurements directly draining heavily polluted areas (for example, mines) or immediately downstream of anthropogenic point sources (for example, wastewater discharge) were excluded, since concentrations from such areas typically exceed riverine concentrations by two to three orders of magnitude, leading to overestimation of the global flux. Samples of all the data were collected at or near river mouths with limited tidal impacts, and data from inland regions were not included in the database. We further avoided using Hg data from the literature that did not provide the sampling site.

Data on global river water discharge and sediment flux were derived from five hydrological datasets, namely Global Water Balance and Transport Model (WBM, 156 watersheds divided based on the COSCAT segmentation scheme), Global Nutrient Export from Watersheds (Global NEWS; 0.5° × 0.5° resolution), Global Flood Awareness System (GloFAS-ERA5; 0.1° × 0.1° resolution), International Satellite Land Surface Climatology Project Initiative II (ISLSCP II; 2.5° × 2.0° resolution) and WBMsed (0.1° × 0.1° resolution). In total, 16 combinations of 4 river water discharge and 4 sediment flux datasets from the above 5 datasets were generated. Grid data from GloFAS-ERA5 and WBMsed were extracted with our developed algorithm in MATLAB. In the algorithm, the location of the discharge in each river was the most downstream point in the river catchment connected to the coastal ocean. Grid data from watersheds with discharges not connecting to or flowing toward any coastal ocean were discarded. The Caspian Sea and Black Sea were included in our estimates. Supplementary Table 2 compares these datasets, which shows that global rivers annually exported 38,949 ± 1,795 (standard deviation) km³ and 15,438 ± 1,068 Tg of freshwater discharge and sediment flux, respectively, to coastal oceans. These results suggested that the datasets are sufficiently similar to each other and would not cause large uncertainties in our estimates.

We computed the global river Hg export (including MeHg) to coastal oceans using geospatial data and tools, and following recently published methods with some modifications. We separated the river Hg load into particulate Hg (Hgᴾ) and dissolved Hg (Hgᴰ). Most riverine Hg is transported to the ocean associated with particles. We estimated the Hgᴾ concentration based on the total suspended sediment (TSS, mg l⁻¹) concentration and anthropogenic Hg emission flux (AE, g km⁻² yr⁻¹) in each ocean basin as divided by Amos et al.:
Hgᴾᵢⱼ = 10^(a × log(TSSᵢⱼ) + b × log(AEᵢⱼ) + c)
where Hgᴾᵢⱼ is the Hgᴾ concentration (ng l⁻¹) in grid j flowing into ocean i and a, b and c are fitting parameters of different multivariate regression models. Previous studies used the water flow rate to estimate the riverine concentration and/or flux of Hg. An increase in rainfall can lead to increases in both water flow and riverine TSS yields. Thus, in regions with low or no human impacts, the fitting method using water flow would not generate largely different results when using the TSS yield (for example, 37–44 Mg yr⁻¹ in previous estimates for pan-Arctic rivers versus 36 Mg yr⁻¹ in the present study). However, in lower latitudes with more human activities (for example, river damming and land-use changes), the riverine TSS yield is also strongly influenced by these human activities. According to the regression models, TSS contributed most (73%) of the variation in river Hgᴾ concentration, followed by anthropogenic Hg emission. These correlations nonetheless offered three important insights: (1) on a global scale, TSS concentration could be a better proxy than the water flow rate for estimating river Hg concentration; (2) impacted by hydrologic events, legacy anthropogenic Hg stored in soils had a stronger impact on the Hg concentration than the primary emissions did; and (3) like carbon, agricultural soil erosion may largely perturb the global river Hg transport. Considering that most river Hg existed in the particulate phase, the relationship between TSS and Hgᴾ suggested that soils could function as a temporary reservoir of anthropogenic Hg and TSS was a benchmark superior to the water flow rate in the global estimate. Legacy releases of Hg into the environment may be a better determinant of riverine export of Hg than present-day Hg emissions. However, the low resolution of current inventories of legacy releases of Hg (at the continental scale) cannot support the establishment of a highly spatially explicit model. In contrast, the present-day inventory of Hg emissions constructed by the UNEP has a relatively high resolution (0.5° × 0.5°) and can partly explain the variation in the riverine Hg concentration. Moreover, most legacy Hg in landmasses is stored in soil and fluvial sediments; thus, the riverine TSS yield can partially represent the legacy effect of Hg.

The river Hgᴰ concentration was positively correlated to the Hgᴾ concentration, water flow (Q, km³ yr⁻¹) and DOC concentration (mg l⁻¹). Hgᴰ concentration estimates were calculated with the following regression model:
Hgᴰᵢⱼ = 10^(a × log(Hgᴾᵢⱼ) + b × log(Qᵢⱼ) + c × log(DOCᵢⱼ) + d)
where d is the intercept. Overall, this approach reproduced 72 and 84% of measured variations in river Hgᴾ and Hgᴰ concentrations, respectively. According to the observational data, global river MeHg concentrations were mostly controlled by the Hg pool (R² = 0.47, P < 0.01**, n = 148), including Hgᴾ and Hgᴰ concentrations (R² = 0.34 and R² = 0.38, respectively, P < 0.01**). The correlation between Hg and MeHg was used to reproduce the level of and variation in the MeHg concentration. Dissolved MeHg (MeHgᴰ) concentration accounted for most of the variation in river MeHg (R² = 0.89, P < 0.01**, n = 113) and a regression was applied to separate the MeHg concentration into particulate MeHg (MeHgᴾ) and MeHgᴰ.

In this study, the six spatially distributed estimates of river Hg and MeHg exports were developed based on WBM and COSCAT (156 data points), Global NEWS (6,081) and WBMsed (45,978). In the estimates, Global NEWS grid DOC concentrations were adopted because of the relatively high resolution (0.5° × 0.5°). A comparison of the inventories showed that the estimate using WBMsed was the closest to the average of all the inventories. Moreover, the correlations between model outputs using WBMsed and field observations suggested that the current modelling strategy could describe the levels and spatial distribution variations in global river Hg exports to coastal oceans.

We used MATLAB to aggregate the spatial distribution of river Hg export into different segmentations, such as watersheds, continents and coastal oceans. We partitioned global coastal oceans into 45 segmentations according to the definition in MARGins and CATchments Segmentation (MARCATS). The water flow direction in each grid and water depths in each coastal ocean were calculated based on a grid-registered topographic map of the global seafloor (1/60° × 1/60° resolution) using ArcMap software. The average depth of each coastal ocean was generated using ArcMap. To obtain the volume of each coastal ocean, we multiplied the resulting average water depth by the water area of each ocean. The boundaries of different ocean basins were referenced from Amos et al.

Statistical analyses were conducted using R software. We used a combination of linear and multivariate analyses to examine the possible effects of different ancillary parameters on different Hg species at global and regional scales. For each fit, the dataset was randomly split into a training set (80% of data) and test set (20% of data) for 1,000 iterations; these sets were defined as samples of data used to fit the model or evaluate a fit result, respectively. A bootstrapping algorithm using the simpleboot package was applied to run all model fits 1,000 times and generate the uncertainties of the fits. Many environmental variables, such as Hg and DOC concentrations, were highly skewed. Thus, a log-normal transformation was applied to normalize variances of Hg concentrations and associated ancillary parameters. We ensured that collinearities between different predicting variables were weak (maximum 25%).

Linear mixed-effect regression models were fitted using the lme4 package. To accommodate any variance associated with geographical distribution, rivers on different continents and flowing into different oceans were treated as random effects in two separate linear mixed-effect regression models. R² (defined as fixed effects only) and R² (including both random and fixed effects), calculated using the MuMin package, were used to compare the relative importance of the two random effects. Models were examined and ranked using the Akaike information criterion (AIC), AICc and AIC weights (percentage of the likelihood of a certain model to the sum of all likelihoods). The significance of the results was determined at the *P < 0.05 and **P < 0.01 levels.

We constructed an empirically constrained mechanistic model for the coastal ocean to investigate the potential impact of riverine Hg exports on Hg cycling using field data collected from published literature, remote-sensing observations and data output from different models. The methods of published literature were used to calculate the transformations between different Hg species (for example, due to absorption, desorption, photo-redox and biological redox) and the exchanges of Hg between the coastal ocean and the atmosphere, open ocean and sediment. The Hg flux of each compartment was represented by a system of coupled first-order differential equations. The Hg inputs from atmospheric deposition, rivers and open-ocean currents drove the model. Because a large timestep could result in the numerical instability of Hg oxidation and reduction, the model was run with a temporal resolution of one hour.

The average water depth of the coastal ocean was calculated using a grid-registered high-resolution topographic map of the global seafloor and ArcMap software. The coastal ocean area was calculated using ArcMap software. Water exchanges between coastal and oceanic waters are poorly represented in current coarse-grid Earth system models. Here, the water exchange of the coastal ocean with the open ocean was calculated using the average residence time of oceanic water in the global coastal ocean, that is, the coastal ocean volume divided by the residence time, and the water flux and Hg concentration in the open ocean were applied to estimate the flux of Hg entering the coastal ocean from the open ocean. The biotic- and photo-oxidation/reduction rates of Hg were calculated following Soerensen et al. Absorption and desorption of Hg in coastal waters were calculated using a stable log partition coefficient for Hg of 5.0 ± 0.39 which was calculated based on recent observations from coastal areas across the Northern Hemisphere. The sedimentation rate of Hg was calculated from the average depth of each coastal ocean, the settling velocity of particles and the simulated particulate Hg concentration in coastal waters. To characterize the interaction between benthic sediment and coastal water, an active-sediment-layer depth was set according to published literature. Other influencing factors, including topography, seawater temperature, wind speed, shortwave radiation, net primary production, chlorophyll, DOC and particle concentration, were also considered in the model.

Here, we conducted a Monte Carlo simulation to estimate the uncertainty ranges of the coastal ocean model. In the model, Hg concentrations in seawater and sediment exhibited log-normal distributions. The distribution types of the rate constants were set as uniform, triangular or normal distribution, depending on the published literature. Standard deviations or ranges of some parameters were not available. In these cases, we assumed a uniform distribution with a fixed variable coefficient of 30%. Finally, we ran the model 10,000 times using MATLAB and generated the median values and interquartile range (25% to 75%) of the modelling results to quantify the uncertainty ranges.

---

# Global habitat hotspots and extinction vulnerability of terrestrial vertebrates

## Abstract
Protecting critical habitats globally is essential to buffer threatened species from extinction due to anthropogenic stressors. However, a limited understanding of the global distribution patterns of habitats for threatened terrestrial vertebrates and their associated habitat hotspots hinders efforts aimed at assessing biodiversity vulnerability and developing targeted conservation strategies. In this study, we integrated spatial data from the International Union for Conservation of Nature's Red List of Threatened Species with a deep learning approach to evaluate the habitat distribution for 4,851 threatened species and construct a global niche interaction network. These habitat hotspots cover only 4% of the terrestrial surface but encompass 67.9% of all analysed species. While vast unoccupied habitats exist, 76.8% of them remain geographically isolated by continental or oceanic barriers and are significantly affected by human pressures. Species at higher risk of extinction often have a larger proportion of unoccupied habitats, but geographic isolation limits these ecological opportunities. We identified regions where unoccupied habitats are concentrated and have long-term persistence potential. Furthermore, the continued loss of native habitats for species inhabiting islands or continental margins due to human pressures may accelerate the widespread homogenization of global niches, underscoring the rarity and irreplaceability of these fragile habitats. Our global assessment provides useful perspectives on sustainable conservation planning.

## Introduction
Although the 36 recognized biodiversity hotspots harbour a large number of endemic terrestrial species and have long been prioritized in global conservation efforts, these regions are still facing increasingly severe threats of habitat loss. Restricted dispersal capabilities, specialized habitat needs and limited physiological tolerances render threatened terrestrial vertebrates highly vulnerable to environmental shifts. According to Hutchinson's niche theory, species occupying different geographic regions may share similar environmental niches, suggesting that vast areas of potentially suitable habitats may exist beyond the current distribution ranges of many species. Certain regions serve as critical hubs for habitat aggregation due to favourable environmental conditions that support the habitation and reproduction of numerous species. In these regions, the habitats of different species spatially converge and overlap, forming habitat 'hotspots' that are characterized by a high degree of niche sharing and ecological interaction. However, gaps in the understanding of global distribution patterns and hotspots of habitats for threatened terrestrial vertebrates limit the effectiveness of conservation planning and strategy development.

With the intensifying pressures of climate change and human activities, critical opportunities for conserving these threatened species may arise not only within established biodiversity hotspots but also beyond their occupied ranges. Despite recognizing these broad trends, our understanding of the global patterns of habitats for threatened species remains limited. Critical questions persist: how much habitat remains for these species worldwide? Of this, what proportion remains unoccupied, and how much is isolated as potential habitat islands by continental or oceanic barriers? How can identifying hotspots across these different habitat components enhance ongoing conservation efforts? Addressing these knowledge gaps could effectively inform targeted conservation actions aimed at mitigating extinction risks and facilitating species persistence.

Over the past century, habitat fragmentation and isolation driven by human pressure and climate change have contributed to major global biodiversity declines, preventing many species from occupying their full environmental niches and leaving extensive potential habitats unfilled due to dispersal limits. For many threatened terrestrial vertebrates, present-day distributions are often remnants shaped by historical geological and climatic events, including continental drift, glacial cycles and tectonic activities. As a consequence, natural barriers and human-modified landscapes further restrict the ability of species to disperse to other environmentally suitable areas, confining them to increasingly fragmented and degraded habitats. Although many studies have assessed habitat distributions across taxa conservation actions often focus on regions exceptionally rich in species diversity and endemism. While this approach yields short-term benefits, such areas frequently represent the final refuges for many endangered species amid ongoing habitat loss.

The spatial disjunction of species with similar ecological requirements highlights a critical conservation blind spot: isolated populations sharing analogous niches may form overlooked ecological clusters that play unique roles in shaping broader meta-ecosystems. Ecological networks offer an interaction-centred perspective that extends beyond patterns detectable in local-area assessments. Ecological networks encapsulate the complex interplay between species and their environments. Modern ecological network studies originated from food web theory, initially emphasizing energy transfer between trophic levels, and have subsequently expanded to encompass mutualistic interactions, host-parasite relationships and habitat networks. Within these networks, perturbations to keystone species may unleash cascading effects, reorganizing ecosystem structures. Therefore, using niche networks to reveal interspecific associations and clustering patterns can help to identify key niche nodes within ecosystems, thereby facilitating a more efficient allocation of conservation resources to these critical areas.

Many studies using species distribution models to assess habitats prioritize data-rich, widely distributed species to ensure reliable predictions, yet this practice often excludes narrowly distributed species that are most vulnerable to extinction under climate change. The standardized spatial data from the International Union for Conservation of Nature's (IUCN) Red List of Threatened Species provide a consistent framework for mapping species distributions, enabling the integration of the current geographic ranges of threatened species at a global scale. This dataset also supports the Aichi Biodiversity Targets and Sustainable Development Goals. However, using these spatial datasets to accurately predict habitat distribution remains a major challenge. Interdisciplinary approaches integrating niche theory are progressively enhancing our ability to tackle such complex projections. Recent advances in deep learning, particularly graph neural networks (GNNs), enable the detection of subtle environmental patterns and improve our ability to characterize how species with distinct ecological preferences interact with complex landscapes.

In this study, we applied a deep learning approach to each of the 4,851 threatened vertebrate species to predict the global distribution of their remaining habitats. By extending the analysis beyond currently occupied ranges, we classified the global habitats of each species into three components: (1) native habitats overlapping existing distributions, (2) unoccupied but environmentally suitable habitats and (3) geographically isolated unoccupied habitats constrained by natural or oceanic barriers, reflecting spatial accessibility constraints. By aggregating predictions across species, we identified global habitat hotspots and evaluated the contributions of these habitat components. We also analysed patterns of habitat isolation and evaluated gaps in protected area coverage. To identify regions with long-term conservation potential, we integrated current unoccupied habitat hotspots with projections of future climate vulnerability, highlighting spatial priorities to ensure sustainable conservation. Finally, we constructed a global niche interaction network to reveal patterns of niche enrichment and the structural shifts driven by habitat loss.

## Results
### Global patterns of habitat hotspots
By predicting global habitat suitability for each of the 4,851 threatened species, we calculated hotspot values through the cumulative overlay of all suitability layers. Areas with high hotspot values are primarily concentrated in Central America, the Andes, the forests of West Africa, the montane regions of East Africa, Madagascar, the Western Ghats and Sri Lanka, the mountains of South China, and the coastal and island regions of Southeast Asia. We identified the spatial boundaries of habitat hotspots by detecting areas with significant clustering of high hotspot values at the 99% confidence level. These hotspot regions encompass 67.9% of all species analysed in this study (3,294 threatened species) while covering only 4% of the terrestrial surface. The hotspots of all three taxonomic groups are primarily distributed in temperate and tropical regions, with distinct spatial differences observed. Although the total hotspot area for mammals is 1.50 times larger than that for amphibians and 1.18 times larger than that for reptiles, mammals have the lowest hotspot peak value globally. For amphibians, the habitat hotspots form a continuous belt extending from Central America to the Andes, representing the region with the greatest overlap in habitat suitability among species. The largest mammal hotspot in the Americas is located in the Amazonian plain. In addition, mammals and reptiles exhibit widespread hotspot regions across West Africa and the Indochinese Peninsula. In Asia, amphibians exhibit multiple small and isolated habitat hotspots, whereas reptiles are primarily concentrated in continuous coastal areas. Moreover, although only 78.1% of these species (3,790 species) are currently found within protected areas, as many as 93.5% (4,536 species) have contiguous native habitats extending into protected areas. This suggests that protected areas play a crucial buffering role in mitigating the loss of native habitats for threatened vertebrates.

To explore the role of landscape patterns in shaping habitat hotspots, we used patch diversity (PD) and degree centrality (DC) to assess landscape diversity and connectivity. Spatial regression was employed to analyse the spatial associations between these landscape characteristics and hotspot values. The DC showed a significant negative association with habitat hotspot values across most hotspot regions (p < 0.05), especially in Central America, southern Europe and southern Asia. This suggests that the most concentrated habitat areas are often located in regions characterized by high landscape fragmentation or peripheral zones with low connectivity. In contrast, the association between PD and hotspot values varied by region. In the Brazilian Plateau, West African forests, East African highlands, Madagascar, the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau, the Indian Peninsula and New Guinea, PD exhibited a significant negative association with hotspot values (local coefficients ranging from −0.2 to −0.76, p < 0.05). This suggests that habitat hotspots in these tropical islands or high-elevation regions are often located in landscapes with lower diversity, reflecting the specialized ecological requirements of species in these areas and their dependence on distinct habitat types. Conversely, PD showed a significant positive association with hotspot values in Central America and the Andes (p < 0.05), indicating that hotspots in these regions are characterized by a mosaic of diverse landscape patches.

### Geographic isolation and vulnerability in global habitats
To evaluate the impact of habitat isolation on the ecological vulnerability of threatened terrestrial vertebrates, we classified each species habitats into two components: native habitats and unoccupied habitats. The latter included isolated habitats, defined as unoccupied areas separated from native habitats by continental or oceanic barriers (hereafter 'isolated habitats'). Native and unoccupied habitats account for 8.4% and 91.6% of the total habitat count for these species, respectively. Amphibians, mammals and reptiles contribute 36.4%, 34.4% and 29.2% of the global habitat count, respectively. In addition, isolated habitats, separated by continental or oceanic barriers, constitute 76.8% of the unoccupied habitats. These isolated habitat hotspots are primarily concentrated in Central America, West Africa, eastern Madagascar, and the coastal and island regions of Southeast Asia. Our analysis revealed that 3,812 species, accounting for 78.6% of the studied species, have unoccupied isolated habitats globally, highlighting the widespread occurrence of habitat isolation among them. In the Americas, isolated habitat hotspots for all three taxonomic groups are predominantly located in Central America. In Africa and Asia, the isolated habitat hotspots of amphibians are spatially consistent with their total habitat hotspots, suggesting that many species occupying different regions share similar environmental niches.

By quantifying the proportional contributions of different habitat components within each grid cell, our analysis revealed that 64% of hotspot areas primarily consist of native habitats as these habitats represent the largest share of total habitat accumulation in these cells. In particular, the Andes, Madagascar and southern China are dominated by native habitat hotspots, suggesting that these areas primarily support the habitat needs of locally endemic species rather than acting as potential dispersal destinations for other species. In contrast, 36% of the hotspot areas are primarily composed of unoccupied habitats, with isolated habitats contributing more than half of the total in 74% of these areas.

There is a clear relationship between species extinction risk and habitat area. Among the three IUCN Red List threat categories, species classified as Endangered (EN) and Critically Endangered (CR) generally have smaller native habitat areas than Vulnerable (VU) species. In addition, the proportion of unoccupied habitats increases with higher extinction risk. Specifically, the mean proportions of unoccupied habitats relative to total habitats are 33.6%, 34.9% and 44.8% for VU, EN and CR species, respectively. This trend is consistent across all taxonomic groups and similarly applies to geographically isolated habitats. We found that regions exposed to high human pressure (Human Footprint Index (HFI) ≥6) corresponded to areas with higher hotspot values. In particular, in areas with hotspot values above the 90th percentile, 58.5% of native habitat hotspots and 59.7% of unoccupied habitat hotspots are under high human pressure. In addition, the unoccupied habitats of CR and EN species experience significantly greater human impacts than those of VU species, as indicated by higher HFI values (Kruskal-Wallis test followed by Dunn's multiple comparisons test, p_adjust < 0.05).

### Environmental niche aggregation of threatened vertebrates
Variations across multiple environmental gradients jointly shape the distinctive patterns of niche clustering in threatened vertebrates. The first two principal component analysis (PCA) axes (PC1 and PC2) for native and unoccupied habitats explained 67.2% and 65.2% of the total environmental variation, respectively. PC1 was primarily driven by annual precipitation (BIO12) and the normalized difference vegetation index (NDVI), with loadings of −0.422 and −0.410 for native habitats and −0.437 and −0.415 for unoccupied habitats. PC2 was mainly associated with topographic variables, including elevation (ELE) and slope (SP), both of which had loadings greater than 0.53 along PC2.

Kernel density estimation was applied to PCA coordinates to identify niche aggregation areas. Nearly all species' niches clustered in regions characterized by stable temperatures (opposite the mean diurnal range (BIO2) axis), highlighting niche conservatism of threatened vertebrates regarding temperature fluctuations. In addition, high percent tree cover (PTC) and drainage density (DD) further contributed to the formation of these niche clusters, directing niche aggregation along their respective variable axes. For native habitats, two distinct niche clusters situated far from the PCA centroid separated along the positive and negative axes of PC2. The niche cluster along the positive axis of PC2 represents species confined to steep mountainous areas characterized by high precipitation and dense tree cover. Conversely, the cluster along the negative PC1 axis corresponds to lowland specialists (opposite the ELE axis) heavily influenced by human activities (along the HFI axis). The unoccupied habitats exhibited a more uniform and broader niche clustering pattern. This suggests that, for species lacking unoccupied habitats, the loss of their native habitats may drive global niche patterns towards more generalized conditions by reducing niche diversity. Moreover, niche aggregation along PC2 in unoccupied habitats spanned temperature gradients and human pressures, indicating that the associated habitats primarily cluster in regions with higher temperatures and elevated human disturbance. Such clustering is particularly unfavourable for species reliant on cool, moist refugia away from human activities.

### Future protection opportunities with persistence potential
By incorporating climate change vulnerability under the Shared Socio-economic Pathway (SSP) 5-8.5 scenario, we identified regions with high concentrations of currently unoccupied habitats projected to maintain suitability for threatened species during the period 2061–2080, highlighting potential opportunities for future conservation. Areas characterized by low future climate change vulnerability and currently high hotspot values of unoccupied habitats are predominantly distributed in the Andes, Central America, East Africa, the Amazon region, southern China and the islands of Southeast Asia. In addition, fragmented regions with high persistence potential were identified in Europe, particularly along its western coasts. These regions are likely to serve as climate refugia, providing essential buffers against habitat loss for threatened species under future climate change. In contrast, unoccupied habitats concentrated in northern South America, West African forests, the eastern Indian Peninsula and mainland Southeast Asia (>0.9 quantile) exhibit high climate vulnerability.

To identify areas with the greatest future conservation benefit for unoccupied habitats under climate change, we conducted a spatial prioritization analysis using the magnitude of climate change as a constraint layer. Our results show that only 59.9% of the prioritized areas with high persistence potential are currently protected by protected areas (PAs), leaving the remaining 40.1% unprotected. Moreover, the HFI values in these unprotected priority regions are significantly higher than in PAs (Student's t-test, p < 0.05). This finding highlights the compounded vulnerability of these critical habitats to both climate change impacts and human disturbances, emphasizing the urgent need to expand protection efforts in these regions to mitigate climate change impacts and safeguard potential future refugia for threatened vertebrates.

### Niche network topology and enrichment patterns
By constructing a network topology based on environmental niche characteristics, we analysed the potential interactions and association structures among the niches of threatened vertebrates. As shown in Fig. 5, different taxonomic groups occupy distinct topological positions within this network, reflecting unique environmental gradients along various niche axes. Compared with the other taxonomic groups, amphibians dominate 65.4% of the niche nodes in the network, reflecting their highly diverse habitat preferences. Branch 5 represents a distinct niche assemblage predominantly occupied by amphibians, including 375 species from regions such as the Andes and the Tibetan Plateau. These species are associated with mountain environments characterized by high elevations and wilderness areas, as indicated by the ELE, SP and HFI variables. In contrast, reptiles and mammals aggregate in distinct, separate network regions, dominating niche nodes with proportions of 25.2% and 9.4%, respectively. Branch 1 primarily comprises 170 island species from Southeast Asian islands and Sri Lanka, of which reptiles, amphibians and mammals account for 52%, 36% and 12%, respectively. Branches 1, 2 and 4 exhibit high levels of human pressure, with Branch 2 experiencing very high pressure (median HFI = 15). Nodes dominated by amphibians and reptiles cluster on opposite sides of the network, indicating that despite varying degrees of geographic overlap among species, multidimensional environmental factors have driven the formation of distinct and complementary niche differentiation patterns.

To independently assess the influence of environmental factors, we conducted Kruskal-Wallis (KW) tests on the three taxonomic groups. Variables such as ELE, SP, DD, aridity index (AI), annual mean temperature (BIO1), BIO12, PTC and HFI were identified as significant contributors, explaining differences in niche network enrichment among these groups (KW test, p < 0.05). The two-sample Kolmogorov-Smirnov (KS) test identified significant factors that explain niche network isolation between specific groups and other species. In networks dominated by amphibians or reptiles, variables, including SP, ELE, AI, PTC, NDVI and DD, significantly differed from those of other species (KS test, p < 0.05). In contrast, threatened mammals occupied only a few scattered niche nodes, indicating high niche overlap between species. In addition, HFI significantly distinguished mammal-dominated nodes from those of other species (KS test, p < 0.05), suggesting that human pressures play a critical role in constraining the ecological niches of mammals.

We used the modularity algorithm to partition the niche network into subsets with distinct community structures. The native habitats of threatened vertebrates exhibit 14 distinct patterns, while unoccupied habitats display only 10. This suggests that although unoccupied habitats occur globally for most species, the native habitats of certain species represent some of the last remaining and rare ecological niches worldwide. This is especially true for branches with unique niches, where the loss of native habitats could lead to the contraction or disappearance of peripheral branches extending outwards within the network (Branches 1–6). Consequently, species in these branches exhibit more pronounced niche conservatism, with their natural habitats being scarce on a global scale. We found that this ecological degradation pattern of declining niche diversity primarily occurs in network nodes dominated by amphibians and reptiles, indicating that habitat loss may place these species at a higher risk of extinction than other taxonomic groups.

## Discussion
In this study, we systematically evaluated the global distribution of habitat hotspots for threatened terrestrial vertebrates and highlighted potential conservation opportunities aligned with sustainable biodiversity targets. Unlike traditional habitat assessments that broadly incorporate dispersal limitations, our approach goes beyond the current distributions of species by deconstructing their habitats into distinct components.

Our analysis indicates that the Panama land bridge is one of the primary habitat hotspots in the New World, serving as a crucial connector between the Neotropical and Nearctic realms and facilitating the formation of suitable migration corridors. This pattern may have provided the ecological conditions that enabled the Great American Biotic Interchange around 3 million years ago. Consistent with previous studies, we found that species with smaller occupied habitat ranges are at a higher risk of extinction. However, our results further show that CR species tend to have a larger proportion of their habitats isolated and inaccessible relative to other species. This pattern is emblematic of a broader trend: while many threatened species have extensive areas of potentially suitable habitat beyond their known ranges, these areas are often fragmented, sparsely distributed and exhibit minimal spatial overlap. The scattered configuration of these habitats presents a major challenge to coordinated conservation efforts and highlights the vulnerability of these species in global conservation planning.

From a sustainability perspective, the global reserve of potential habitats is essential for enhancing long-term resilience and buffering the impacts of climate change, yet both native and unoccupied habitats are being progressively eroded by increasing human pressures. Human pressures have triggered substantial changes in community composition and may lead to community homogenization on a global scale by affecting species dispersal and redistribution, although they often result in heterogenization at the local scale. These human-driven shifts may be linked to habitat alterations. We have demonstrated that, despite the presence of substantial unoccupied habitats for many threatened species, a large portion of these habitats remains inaccessible due to continental and oceanic barriers. For example, we found that Abavorana nazgul, an amphibian species currently endemic to the Malay Peninsula, also has potentially suitable but discrete habitats in the southern tip of Sumatra, the northern coastal areas of Borneo and the southern edge of New Guinea. Consequently, dispersal limitations may hinder species exchange and gene flow across regions, allowing these communities to maintain their independence. Furthermore, our niche network analysis revealed that the continuous loss of native habitats, driven by human impacts, often homogenizes global habitat conditions by reducing niche diversity. This process is closely tied to the unique niche spaces concentrated along islands and continental margins, which represent vulnerable and globally irreplaceable ecological resources. Ensuring sustainable outcomes requires a reduction in the conflicts between land-use expansion and habitat integrity while actively limiting human impacts in areas with high potential to sustain biodiversity and advance conservation goals.

While climate change may create climatically suitable areas in certain regions, as widely assessed in earlier studies, the ecological conditions necessary for terrestrial vertebrates to sustainably occupy and persist in these habitats extend far beyond mere climatic suitability. Existing habitats capable of supporting diverse communities result from long-term ecological interactions involving vegetation dynamics, soil formation, hydrological regimes and anthropogenic influences. Under rapid climate change scenarios, these factors often fail to synchronize adequately with shifting climatic conditions. For instance, some tropical forest-dependent birds rely heavily on mature canopy structures, while certain large mammals require a mosaic landscape of grasslands and shrub patches to facilitate migration and resource acquisition. As a result of limited ecological resilience and regeneration capacity, many regions that rapidly achieve suitable climatic conditions may nonetheless experience considerable lag times before their habitat conditions can fully meet the specialized ecological requirements of species. This ecological inertia or lag effect underscores a critical point: rapid climate change does not automatically translate into the rapid availability of suitable habitats. Thus, in this study, we explicitly captured multiple dimensions of conservation by integrating current critical habitat opportunities with assessments of future climate vulnerabilities. This allowed us to identify hotspot regions that are most likely to remain suitable and sustain threatened species persistence under future climate change.

Habitat loss outside PAs is projected to exceed that within PAs, potentially leading to the formation of community aggregation zones at the edges of PAs. We have shown that if the habitats currently occupied by these threatened species are substantially reduced, their future dispersal centres may shift towards areas with more intensive human activity, which could, in turn, drive some species away from PA boundaries. However, although existing PAs buffer many native habitats for EN species, a large amount of unoccupied habitat with high future persistence potential remains outside these areas, facing much higher human pressures. We predict that neglecting these unoccupied habitats, which already contain well-integrated ecological resources, may weaken their ability to compensate for the loss of original habitats under changing climate conditions and thus reduce overall conservation effectiveness.

We advocate incorporating habitat hotspot regions into a multifaceted conservation strategy that integrates ecological and socio-economic factors to improve conservation effectiveness. Such measures are especially critical for species with narrow ecological niches or limited dispersal capacities, which are disproportionately susceptible to habitat loss and fragmentation. For example, building on native habitats, the inclusion of additional high-potential unoccupied habitat clusters offers a pathway to expand the current PA network. Linking these currently underused but widespread habitats can enhance the role of isolated areas as enduring refugia, bolstering genetic diversity and mitigating the effects of geographic isolation. By integrating the distribution of these key habitat resources, we can not only evaluate existing conservation priorities but also uncover overlooked opportunities for habitat restoration and connectivity planning. This integrated approach reorients conservation from static preservation to a forward-looking strategy designed to bolster ecosystem resilience under intensifying ecological stressors and land-use transformation.

## Methods
### Focal species and data compilation
We obtained distribution range data for 4,851 threatened terrestrial vertebrates from the IUCN Red List website, including 979 mammals, 2,433 amphibians and 1,439 reptiles. Our study focused on species classified as Vulnerable (VU), Endangered (EN) or Critically Endangered (CR). We used these data to estimate global habitat suitability for each species instead of relying on presence point data. While presence points are effective for fine-scale analyses, their application to a global assessment involving thousands of threatened species is constrained by inconsistent coverage, uneven sampling intensity and spatial biases, such as under-sampling in remote regions and over-representation in accessible areas. These limitations may affect the stability of the estimates and lead to increased uncertainty when results are aggregated across species. In contrast, IUCN range polygons provide standardized, expert-validated spatial data that synthesize occurrence records, habitat preferences and ecological insights, adhering to rigorous mapping protocols. This standardized format ensures consistency in distribution information across thousands of species, facilitating uniform comparisons of habitat suitability among diverse taxonomic groups and geographic regions while mitigating biases inherent in presence points and supporting robust global assessments.

To assess the global habitat distribution of each species, we evaluated habitat suitability using key environmental variables relevant to their ecological requirements, guided by climate stability and habitat heterogeneity hypotheses. For current climatic conditions, we used 19 bioclimatic variables from WorldClim v2.1 (1970–2000 average) at 30-arcsec resolution as the baseline reference. Of these, four representative variables, namely, annual mean temperature (BIO1), mean diurnal range (BIO2), annual precipitation (BIO12) and precipitation seasonality (BIO15), were used to model habitat suitability. Together, these variables capture the prevailing thermal and moisture conditions, as well as their contemporary variability across terrestrial environments. To assess climate change vulnerability, we obtained future projections of the same bioclimatic variables from WorldClim v2.1 under the SSP5-8.5 scenario, averaged for the period 2061–2080 at 30-arcsec resolution. Elevation (ELE) data were obtained from WorldClim v2.1 and used to derive the global slope (SP). The aridity index (AI), representing the 1970–2000 mean and calculated as a function of precipitation and reference evapotranspiration, was obtained from the Global Aridity Index and Potential Evapotranspiration (ET0) Climate Database v2 at 30-arcsec resolution. We obtained global river network data from the HydroRIVERS database and calculated drainage density (DD) through line density estimation. The normalized difference vegetation index (NDVI) was derived from the GIMMS NDVI3g v1 dataset at a spatial resolution of 1/12 degree. PTC data, representing the proportion of land covered by trees, were obtained in a gridded format at 15-arcsec resolution. The HFI at 1-km resolution for 2009, provided by the Socioeconomic Data and Applications Center (SEDAC), quantifies the cumulative human impact on the environment, with values ranging from 0 (no pressure) to 50 (very high pressure). All geospatial data have been converted to the World Geodetic System 1984 (WGS1984) coordinate system with 30-arcsec (~1-km) resolution. Although not perfectly synchronized due to the dynamic nature of dataset updates, these data collectively provide a robust contemporary snapshot of environmental conditions. By integrating them into a unified modelling framework, our approach captures the average ecological state of the current period, minimizing the influence of short-term discrepancies. As habitat suitability assessments are primarily driven by broader ecological patterns rather than transient fluctuations, this integration ensures a consistent and reliable foundation for our global evaluations.

PA locations and boundary data were obtained from the World Database on Protected Areas (WDPA) as of April 2023. In accordance with the WDPA User Manual and our focus on terrestrial species, we excluded PAs designated as Marine or Proposed. Due to the exclusion of most PA data for China in this WDPA release, we supplemented the dataset by integrating the 2018 boundary data for Chinese PAs from the Resource and Environmental Science Data Platform into the WDPA layer to ensure complete global coverage.

### Global prediction of habitat suitability
To evaluate habitat suitability for all threatened terrestrial vertebrates included in our study using the distribution range data obtained from the IUCN, we incorporated a deep learning approach into the species distribution modelling workflow. This approach involved two main steps. First, environmental grids were transformed into a patch matrix that represents the global landscape. Second, a deep learning method operating on the graph domain was used to infer habitat suitability based on this landscape representation and the known distribution ranges of each species.

Specifically, we used an unsupervised clustering algorithm, known as a self-organizing map, to derive a landscape patch matrix from the environmental raster layers, reflecting localized habitat structures and their associated environmental conditions. This method preserves the topological structure of the data while projecting high-dimensional features onto a lower-dimensional space. Our aim was to group grid cells with similar environmental characteristics into homogeneous polygons, each representing a small habitat patch with distinct ecological properties, and to mosaic them together to form a complete landscape matrix. This analysis was implemented using the kohonen package in R. The aggregated grid cells were then converted into spatial polygon objects, producing a global landscape matrix composed of adjacent patches of varying shapes and sizes, which facilitated direct alignment with species' distribution ranges. This step generated the input matrix required for subsequent habitat suitability prediction using a GNN, which needs to be trained on graph-based data. When a species' distribution range intersects with a landscape patch, the species is considered to potentially occur within that patch. The ecological features of each patch were represented by a vector of mean environmental values computed within its boundaries.

Using the patch matrix that integrates species distribution information and multidimensional environmental features, we independently trained GraphSAGE, a graph sampling aggregation-based GNN model, for each species to predict the global distribution of its suitable habitats. Graph sampling aggregation is a deep learning technique designed for inductive learning, enabling efficient and scalable representation learning on large graphs. It allows the model to account for irregular landscape interactions and to capture the complex niche signals embedded in species adapted to specific habitat conditions across regions. We designated the patch subsets that were spatially consistent with species' distributions as core habitats, capturing the resource conditions associated with their established ranges. The GNN then aggregated feature information from each patch and its neighbouring patches through iterative message-passing and backpropagation to estimate the suitability of all patches across the study area relative to the target habitat. Model training and prediction were performed using the PyTorch Geometric Python package, with the Adam optimizer employed to minimize the error loss function and the LeakyReLU activation function used to calculate the output vector for each layer of the neural network. The generated habitat suitability maps have values ranging from 0 (low suitability) to 1 (high suitability).

### Identification of hotspots across habitat components
The habitat hotspot value at any location was calculated as the cumulative sum of habitat suitability predictions for all species at that location. Hotspot regions were delineated by identifying areas with significant clustering of high hotspot values at a 99% confidence level using the Getis-Ord Gi* hotspot analysis. The distribution of isolated habitat hotspot values was further quantified by overlaying habitat suitability predictions for areas beyond the current distribution ranges of each species, located in distinct land units that are naturally separated by continents and oceans. This approach enabled the identification of hotspot regions formed by these isolated habitats. A set of landscape metrics was calculated to analyse the spatial patterns of habitat hotspots and assess their isolation and fragmentation. These metrics were computed using the FRAGSTATS 4.2.1 software.

To delineate the habitat distribution ranges of each species and classify them into native, unoccupied and geographically isolated habitats, we converted continuous habitat suitability maps into binary (suitable/unsuitable) maps by determining a threshold for each species. Given that the boundary between low and high suitability is often ambiguous, we applied an anomaly detection method based on points of curvature change using the PersistAD anomaly detection model from the ADTK package in Python. By ranking all grid cells by their suitability values and generating a suitability curve, this approach identifies a distinct inflection point where suitability values exhibit a sudden increase, marking a significant transition from low to high suitability. This inflection point represents a species-specific habitat suitability threshold, which we used to classify habitat into suitable and unsuitable areas. We extracted all suitable areas identified for each species and further categorized them based on their overlap with the species' occupied range. Habitats overlapping with a species' extant distribution ranges (from the IUCN Red List) were classified as native habitats, whereas suitable areas beyond these ranges were designated as unoccupied habitats, indicating regions not currently occupied by the species.

To assess habitat isolation, we identified unoccupied habitat areas that were spatially disconnected from the species' current distribution ranges due to continental or oceanic barriers. We partitioned the global land surface into discrete, naturally isolated units using the World Continents dataset. This dataset was refined to separate landmasses based on oceanic boundaries while preserving continental integrity and excluding artificial subdivisions (for example, administrative borders), ensuring that isolation reflected natural geographic discontinuities. For each species, unoccupied habitats located on a different land unit from its extant distribution were classified as geographically isolated habitats, representing areas that are difficult to access due to natural barriers.

### Mapping future persistence potential of unoccupied habitats
To assess the persistence potential of unoccupied habitat hotspots under future climate change as potential refugia or restoration opportunities for species, we integrated climate projections from the Coupled Model Intercomparison Project Phase 6 into our analysis, focusing on the SSP5-8.5 scenario. This high-emission, fossil-fuel-intensive pathway represents a plausible upper bound of future climate impacts, allowing us to assess the vulnerability of habitat hotspots to climatic shifts. We used all 19 bioclimatic variables from WorldClim v2.1 to quantify the magnitude of future climate change. These variables collectively capture a comprehensive range of climatic factors influencing habitat suitability. We quantified future climate change magnitude by calculating the Euclidean distance between the current (baseline: 1970–2000 average) and future (2061–2080 average) values of all variables for each grid cell. We then used the PCA loadings of these variables as weights to derive a linear combination that reflects the baseline climate background, which was added to the climate change magnitude to represent the projected shift along the dominant climatic gradient under the high-emission scenario. A bivariate map was then created by combining climate change magnitude with unoccupied habitat hotspot values using a two-colour gradient scheme. This map delineates the joint spatial pattern of unoccupied habitat hotspots and their vulnerability to future climatic shifts.

To further analyse gaps in the current PAs and strengthen the actionable insights for guiding conservation prioritization, we employed Zonation 5, a widely respected spatial prioritization tool that integrates ecological and pressure factors. In this analysis, we used the climate change magnitude (from the current period to 2070) as a constraint layer, representing the vulnerability of regions to future climatic shifts. We then incorporated our unoccupied habitat hotspot map as a feature layer to assess prioritization across the landscape. Zonation 5 assigned high priority values to concentrated areas of unoccupied habitats that are less affected by climate change (above the 0.9 quantile of hotspot values). We extracted the boundaries of areas with priority values greater than 50% to represent regions that are most favourable for ensuring the long-term stability and concentrated protection of unoccupied habitats. These high-priority areas were then overlaid with global PAs to assess the current protection status and identify conservation gaps.

### Topological representations for niche networks
Topological data analysis (TDA) offers a powerful tool for analysing complex ecological networks. The Mapper algorithm in TDA relies on specific dimensionality reduction techniques to create a low-dimensional network structure of the data, preserving the connectivity of the information. We used this method to reveal how species overlap and connect across the environmental niches represented by different habitats. TDA was implemented using the Python kmapper package, and enrichment maps of niche networks were visualized in Gephi 0.10. t-Distributed stochastic neighbour embedding (t-SNE) was used as the filter function for data embedding, retaining pairwise distances from the environmental variable matrix in two-dimensional space. The filter range was divided into overlapping bins, with the degree of overlap among the bins controlled by resolution and gain parameters. k-Means clustering was then applied to each bin to identify clusters of species with similar niches and generate network nodes. The variables used for TDA modelling included all environmental features involved in the habitat suitability modelling. Each node in the network represents a group of species occupying similar niche spaces, while each edge indicates the degree of niche similarity between these nodes. The average values of variables and the proportion of taxa are mapped onto the TDA network for rapid visualization and interpretation of niche enrichment. Nodes are coloured according to their environments and principal components. We used the modularity algorithm in Gephi 0.10 to identify the niche network as subsets with distinct community structures. KW tests and two-sample KS tests were used to compare different taxonomic groups and environmental variables, analysing the roles of various factors in regulating niche network enrichment at the global scale.

---

# Ecological risk assessment of marine plastic pollution

## Abstract
Plastic pollution poses serious ecological risks to marine organisms through various pathways, yet a comprehensive risk assessment is lacking. Here we assess the global risks of plastic ingestion, entanglement, pollutant adsorption (methylmercury, MeHg; perfluorooctane sulfonate, PFOS) and additive leaching (bisphenol A; phthalate esters) by integrating a marine plastic model and multi-size marine organism data, as well as MeHg and PFOS datasets. Our analysis reveals that ingestion risks vary with the body size of organisms, and are influenced by both biomass distribution and plastic concentration patterns. Entanglement hotspots align with regions of flourishing coastal fisheries, highlighting a substantial threat to marine species. Ingestion risks and toxicity from leached additives are concentrated in the mid-latitude North Pacific Ocean, mid-latitude Atlantic Ocean and northern Indian Ocean. Plastics exhibit high adsorption of PFOS in the North Atlantic and East and Southeast Asian coasts (0.1–0.3 pg m⁻²) and of MeHg in the northern Indian and southwestern Atlantic oceans (1–18 pg m⁻²). Using future emission scenarios, we project plastic concentrations and estimate reduced risks under emission control strategies. These findings emphasize the urgent need for targeted mitigation efforts and policy interventions to curb escalating impacts of plastic pollution on marine ecosystems.

## Introduction
Marine plastic pollution has emerged as one of the most pressing global environmental challenges. Each year, millions of tons of plastics enter the ocean from rivers, coastlines, shipping and fishing. These plastics disperse throughout the marine environment, reaching ocean gyres and even polar regions, and pose serious threats to marine ecosystems.

The ecological impacts of marine plastic pollution are complex and multifaceted. Numerous studies have documented plastic ingestion by marine organisms. Plastic debris has been identified in the gastrointestinal tracts of diverse marine species, including fish, turtles, seabirds and other fauna. Ingesting plastics can lead to various detrimental effects, such as intestinal blockage and false satiety. These biological consequences extend to altered feeding behaviour, reduced body condition and increased mortality. The probability of ingestion risk is intricately linked to numerous factors such as habitat, body size and ingestion rate. There is also a positive correlation between the body length of marine animals and the maximum size of plastic they are capable of ingesting. For example, the standard length of fish is positively associated with the number of particles found in the gastrointestinal tract.

Plastics can entangle the necks and limbs of wildlife, causing behavioural disturbances and physical injuries, which may be more lethal than ingestion, as evidenced by numerous field observations. Various types of plastic, including ropes, bags, tyres and especially abandoned, lost and discarded fishing gear (ALDFG, known as ghost fishing), contribute substantially to the entanglement risk. The expansion of fishing activities in recent years has accelerated the prevalence of ghost gear, posing heightened threats to marine mammals, reptiles and elasmobranchs. Such a risk shows an age preference, with younger animals being more susceptible to entanglement due to curiosity. Records indicate that organisms experiencing plastic entanglements vary in length, ranging from 25 cm (sea turtles) to over 2 m (whales and seals).

Plastics possess the capability to adsorb organic pollutants and metals owing to their hydrophobic nature and large specific surface area. The sorption behaviour is contingent upon factors such as polymer type, particle size and seawater temperature. Persistent toxic pollutants such as methylmercury (MeHg) and perfluorooctane sulfonate (PFOS) can adsorb onto plastics, forming complexes that are susceptible to ingestion by marine organisms and subsequent transfer through trophic levels within food webs. Notably, smaller plastic particles tend to accumulate higher concentrations of PFOS than their larger counterparts, probably due to their greater specific surface area. Consequently, plastic debris serves as a vector for the horizontal and vertical transport of adsorbed pollutants in the ocean, broadening the spatial extent of exposure. Moreover, plastic degradation releases additive chemicals such as bisphenol A (BPA) and phthalate esters (PAEs), which are used as ultraviolet stabilizers and plasticizers, respectively. These leached chemicals, classified as endocrine disruptors, may impair organismal growth and metabolism, and potentially induce genetic mutations. Current risk assessments are limited by narrow taxonomic focus, restricted geographic scope and incomplete characterization of exposure pathways.

In this Article we propose a comprehensive and scalable framework for assessing the ecological risks of marine plastics at the global scale by quantifying four major exposure pathways: ingestion, entanglement, pollutant adsorption and additive leaching. We utilize plastic concentration outputs from the Nanjing University Marine Plastic (NJU-MP) model within the Massachusetts Institute of Technology general circulation model (MITgcm) framework, which simulates plastic emission, transport and sinking processes, constrained by observational datasets. We also incorporate global marine biomass data from the NEMO-PISCES-APECOSM model, which resolves 20 organism size classes for upper trophic levels. To evaluate pollutant exposure, we use simulated distributions of MeHg and PFOS, selected for their toxicity, persistence and well-characterized physicochemical properties. Leaching risks are estimated for common additives such as BPA and PAEs, on the basis of literature-derived data, their widespread use and ecological impacts. Due to the absence of quantitative dose-effect relationships specific to marine plastics, we evaluate relative risk probabilities rather than absolute risk levels.

## Ingestion risk
The global pattern of plastic ingestion risk varies for organisms of different body sizes. We calculate ingestion risk probability by multiplying organism biomass by plastic concentrations within ingestible size ranges, followed by zonal summation and normalization to reveal latitudinal patterns. For epipelagic organisms (2–823 mm) ingestion risk peaks at mid-latitudes (30–50°) and declines in tropical regions (<20°), reflecting the contrasting distributions of biomass and plastics. Modelled plastics accumulate in Asian coastal zones and oligotrophic subtropical gyres, driven by high emissions and surface transport processes such as Ekman convergence and tidal stranding along coastlines. Conversely, biomass is concentrated in eutrophic zones such as current margins, tropical waters and high latitudes. Elevated nutrient availability and phytoplankton productivity in these areas are linked to vertical processes, including upwelling, mixed-layer shoaling and turbulent mixing, which are generally weak in subtropical gyres. These differing spatial patterns are supported by significant negative spatial correlations between surface plastic concentrations and nutrient levels (phosphate, nitrate and chlorophyll a) across most central subtropical gyres.

The hotspots of modelled ingestion risk are identified in both hemispheres, with particularly high levels in the mid-latitude North Pacific and North Atlantic oceans. Specifically, the northwestern Atlantic exhibits notable ingestion risk for small organisms, contrasting with a heightened risk for large organisms in the northeastern Atlantic. In the northern Indian Ocean, vertical heterogeneity in plastic distribution leads to high ingestion probabilities for epipelagic organisms in the Bay of Bengal and mesopelagic organisms in the Arabian Sea. In the equatorial Pacific Ocean, mesopelagic organisms face ingestion risks three orders of magnitude higher than do epipelagic and migratory organisms with a body size exceeding 823 mm. In the Southern Hemisphere, higher plastic ingestion risk occurs in the South Atlantic Ocean, the southern Indian Ocean and the upwelling zones (Peruvian cold currents). Although plastic loads are low in these upwelling regions, the abundance of organisms contributes to high risk. Our model reveals that plastic garbage patches and biomass hotspots do not always overlap. In the South Pacific, plastics accumulate around 30°S, whereas organisms are concentrated in the range 30–60°S. As a result, ingestion risk remains low despite the presence of a garbage patch, potentially mitigating the ecological impact. In the Northern Hemisphere, the largest organisms (approximately 2 m) face substantial ingestion risk, particularly in the Arctic Ocean, a key habitat with high biomass of large-bodied species that further elevates this risk. The polar branch of the thermohaline circulation has the potential to transport plastics from lower latitudes to the Arctic region, facilitating their sinking and subsequent accumulation. This mechanism raises notable ecological concern. Field observations confirm that fish in the Arctic Ocean are ingesting plastic polymers, including polyethylene (PE) and polyvinyl chloride (PVC), in diverse sizes and colours.

We sum the ingestion risk and multiply it by 10⁸ to generate a comparable risk index and assess the contributions of various plastic types to organism groups. Mesopelagic organisms, boasting the largest biomass, contribute the highest total ingestion risk (176), followed by epipelagic organisms (108) and migratory species (50). The highest risk index is 14 for mesopelagic organisms with a body size of 18 mm. Organisms of 1,203 mm have the lowest ingestion risk index (3.2). For epipelagic and migratory groups, peak risk occurs at a body size of 263 mm, with indices of 8.9 and 3.9, respectively. The contribution ratio of different plastic types is influenced by their size distribution and abundance in seawater. Model results suggest that PVC and polystyrene (PS), within the 0.078–0.3 mm size range, are the predominant components in the surface ocean, with global masses of 3.5 × 10⁸ kg and 2.7 × 10⁸ kg, respectively. Consequently, for smaller organisms, PVC poses the highest risk (45.5%), followed by PS (35.7%) and acrylonitrile butadiene styrene (ABS) (14.5%). As organism size increases, the combined contribution of polypropylene (PP) and PE exceeds 50%, consistent with field observations of dominant ingestion by fish ranging from 291 to 707 mm. This pattern probably reflects the lower densities of these polymers relative to seawater, which promote their retention near the surface and constrain vertical transport. In contrast, denser polymers such as PVC, PS and ABS are more prone to sinking and accumulating at greater depths.

## Entanglement risk
We assess plastic entanglement risk as the product of macroplastic abundance and organism biomass, representing the probability of interaction between large-bodied organisms (>180 mm) and macroplastics (>5 cm) originating from fishing and shipping sources, such as ALDFG. The modelled risk suggests two peaks at 30°N and 30°S with a pronounced decline at the equator, reflecting the latitudinal distribution of biomass and plastic concentrations.
