After filtering and aggregation, cover-abundance information was available for 37 taxa at 12,911 survey sites. The dry meadows and pastures initiative was run by the Swiss Federal Office for Environment and consists of cover-abundance information for almost 24,000 surveyed grassland communities. We focused on 91 graminoid taxa that are dominant and influence the appearance of at least one grassland, wetland or cropland habitat type in Switzerland, according to the TypoCH vegetation classification, and conducted the same aggregation and filtering steps as for the surveys from the Swiss forest vegetation database. After processing, cover-abundance information was available for 23,919 surveys.

Expert-based surveys from the Swiss Biodiversity Monitoring Program (BDM), and from the National Forest Inventory (NFI) were used for independent validation. Survey data from the BDM program were used to validate predicted distributions of individual species and community composition. We considered the BDM indicator Z9 (species diversity in habitats), containing 1562 sites that are regularly distributed across Switzerland. Between 2000 and 2021 each of these 10 m² areas was revisited three to four times and all plant taxa present were recorded. Taxa were mostly identified at the species level, but identifications at the level of species aggregates were also common. We considered a taxon present at a site if it was observed in at least one revisitation. Where applicable, we pooled species to aggregates, yielding 1489 survey sites with at least one species or species aggregate observed and full environmental data coverage, and 1345 taxa observed across the survey sites. Survey data from the NFI were used to validate predicted potentially dominant tree species. The NFI samples forests on a regular grid with 1.4 km spacing (where forests occur). The sampling procedure includes the species-level identification of all trees with ≥12 cm stem diameter at breast height in an inner circle covering 200 m² and the identification of all trees with ≥36 cm diameter at breast height within an outer circle covering 500 m². We considered survivor trees from the third (2004–2006) to the fourth (2009–2017) assessment and defined the species with most individuals with ≥12 cm stem diameter at breast height to be dominant. After filtering and pooling for the same 37 species and species aggregates as distinguished by the Swiss forest vegetation database, information on dominant tree taxa at 1867 sites was available.

**Environmental predictors.** We constructed a low-resolution and a high-resolution set of environmental predictors, representing vegetation structure, climate, soil conditions, topography, and season. Two separate sets were necessary to allow for a fair comparison between multispecies DNNs and stacked SDMs (which were trained in 2019), and to include the best data available for the high-resolution analysis. The low-resolution (100 × 100 m) set included 24 environmental variables, projected in the CH1903/LV03 projection system, and two predictors representing season. The low-resolution environmental layers originated from two recent studies investigating the distribution of Swiss plant species. The predictor group representing vegetation structure included canopy height, inner forest density, and mean and standard deviation of the normalized difference vegetation index (NDVI). Canopy height and inner forest density were estimated by the 25th and 95th height percentiles of normalized LiDAR returns above 40 cm. Mean and standard deviation of NDVI were calculated from Landsat measurements taken during summer months (July to mid-September) between 2007 and 2015. Climate variables describe temperature, precipitation, and solar radiation (direct and diffuse) and were downscaled from reanalysis data to the EarthEnv-DEM90 digital elevation model, using the CHELSA approach. For temperature and precipitation, we considered averages and sums, respectively, for June to August, December to February, and for the whole year. For solar radiation, we only considered annual averages. In addition, we included CHELSA-based estimates of frost change frequency and growing degree days (with 5°C baseline temperature) and plant indicator value-based estimates of continentality and light availability as climatic predictors. Soil conditions are represented by plant indicator value-based estimates of pH, moisture, moisture variability, aeration, humus, and nutrients. Topography variables included terrain wetness index, terrain ruggedness index, and terrain position index, and were derived from the EarthEnv-DEM90 digital elevation model. Finally, we used sine-cosine mapping to encode the season of observation (day of year) into two circular variables, one of them roughly indicating day length and the other change in day length per day. Collinearity in this set of predictors overall was rather low, except for some predictors related to temperature and, to a lesser degree, precipitation. In the subsequent analyses predictors were subsampled in order to avoid pairs with high correlations.

The high-resolution (25 × 25 m) predictor set roughly covered the same predictor groups but included some additional variables. High-resolution variables were compiled in the CH1903/LV95 projection system. For vegetation structure, we aggregated the countrywide vegetation height model with 1 m original horizontal resolution to estimate maximum and 25th percentile of vegetation height in each 25 × 25 m cell. In addition, we calculated annual medians and seasonal differences in enhanced vegetation index (EVI) for the period 2018–2021. EVI data was calculated based on 10 × 10 m Sentinel 2 measurements and compiled to analysis-ready annual and seasonal medians by the Swiss Data Cube. We removed values outside the valid range (−1 to 1), and reprojected and aggregated the layers to 25 × 25 m by average. Then, we aggregated annual, summer, and winter medians inter-annually by median and calculated the difference between summer and winter median. For a handful of pixels, no valid EVI measurements were available, so we approximated them with bilinear interpolation. We also considered data on canopy mixture (deciduous versus evergreen) in forested areas at 25 × 25 m original resolution. We encoded this information as two variables, one representing deciduous forest cover and one representing evergreen forest cover, setting the values for unforested areas to zero. Related to vegetation structure, we also included information on land cover at 25 × 25 m original resolution, encoding the 62 classes distinguished individually as binary factors (one hot encoding). High-resolution temperature and precipitation data were derived from the CHclim25 data set. These data were calculated with topography-dependent interpolation of station measurements to 25 m resolution. From the monthly climatologies for the period 1981–2010 we derived annual means and sums, for temperature and precipitation respectively, as well as annual ranges. Moreover, we calculated precipitation sums for the summer months (June to August). Terrain variables were derived by aggregating the SwissAlt3D model, distributed by the Swiss Federal Office of Topography, from the original 2 m resolution to 25 m by average. From the elevation data (not used as predictor) we calculated aspect as well as the SAGA wetness index (using SAGA GIS). In addition, we calculated the terrain ruggedness index for each 25 × 25 m cell (maximum minus minimum elevation across the covered 2 × 2 m cells). To represent solar radiation, soil conditions, and season, we used the same variables as in the low-resolution set, downscaling them, where applicable, with bilinear interpolation. Collinearity in the high-resolution predictor set was low, except for the association of mean annual temperature with annual temperature range (r = 0.74) and the association between annual precipitation and summer precipitation (r = 0.89).

### Model configurations
**Multispecies deep neural networks.** We trained multispecies deep neural networks at low (100 × 100 m) and high (25 × 25 m) resolution. DNNs were set up with an input layer of one node per environmental feature, four residual blocks with twice a linear layer of 380 nodes followed by a rectified linear unit and a dropout layer in between, and an output layer with one node for each of the 2477 species and species aggregates. For low-resolution DNNs, we sub-selected ten environmental predictors from the low-resolution set, as this was also the number used by SDMs (if sufficient presence observations existed for the modeled species) and to limit model complexity when projecting to future conditions. The primary criteria thereby were variable importance in species distribution models, and absolute Pearson correlation coefficients of no more than 0.7. Yet, we made one exception by choosing summer temperature over solar radiation, despite somewhat lower variable importance, to be able to better account for climate change effects. For the high-resolution DNN, we included the whole set of environmental predictors, as (1) only two predictor pairs with absolute Pearson correlation coefficients >0.7 existed, and (2) we did not use the high-resolution DNN for future projections. All predictor variables were rescaled to range from −1 to 1 prior to analysis. For low-resolution DNNs, we conducted a sensitivity analysis with an alternative predictor set consisting of environmental predictors only (no seasonal predictors). We trained all models for a first phase of 50 epochs with equal sampling probabilities of training observations for each taxon (by upsampling rare species), and then for second phase with unmodified sampling probabilities. The number of epochs considered for this second phase was inferred from a preliminary validation run using the low-resolution models. For high-resolution DNNs, we chose an initial learning rate of 5 × 10⁻³ for training in both phases, while for low-resolution DNNs it was 10⁻² and 5 × 10⁻³, for the first and the second training phase, respectively. In all cases training was optimized with the stochastic gradient descent method and learning rates were reduced when the loss was plateauing, setting the momentum to 0.9. The batch size was set to 250. DNNs were fitted in the python environment using the PyTorch library, the pytorchlib library, the pandas library, and the numpy library. Training times for low-resolution models were about 76 h and 13 h for the NDCG and the CEL cost functions, respectively, when using a Nvidia RTX 3090 GPU.

**Cost functions.** We used two cost functions to train DNNs. As a baseline, we used the cross-entropy loss (CEL). The CEL function is a standard cost function for multiclassification with deep neural networks. The CEL is computationally efficient and has the attractive property of being a strictly proper scoring function that has a unique minimum when the model predicts true conditional probabilities (which are species-specific observation probabilities under the condition that a species presence has been reported at a certain spatiotemporal location). However, the CEL is designed for classification problems with mutually exclusive classes, such as vegetation types, and is minimized, at the level of an individual observation, when the full probability mass is allocated to the observed class. When confronted with opportunistic citizen science observations, where multiple species are expected to present but we only have information about the presence of one of them (and no information about the presence or absence of all other species), the CEL implicitly assumes that all but the observed species are absent. This assumption may be correct for most of the species from the Swiss flora at each spatiotemporal location, but it is not for the species that are present but not reported. While employing the CEL to this type of data may still lead to fits that perform reasonably well, cost functions that less strictly reject unobserved classes have been shown to be advantageous.

The second cost function we used, the normalized discounted cumulative gain (NDCG), makes no assumption about unobserved classes. Instead, it recasts the learning problem as finding the most relevant identity of a species that has been observed at a given spatiotemporal location, a common task in many data science applications, although less common in ecological modeling. Specifically, we employed the LambdaNDCGloss function of the pytorchlib module, which is defined as
l(s, y) = −∑_{i=1}^n ∑_{j=1}^n log₂ c_{n_i, n_j},
where n is the highest rank evaluated and
c_{n_i, n_j} = (1 / (1 + e^(−(s_{n_i} − s_{n_j}))))^(G_{n_i} / D_{n_j}),
with n_i and n_j being indices of the items at rank i and j, respectively. s is a vector of scores returned by the model and y is a vector of relevance labels. Finally,
G_{n_i} = (2^{y_{n_i}} − 1) / max DCG
with max DCG being the maximum discounted cumulative gain, and D_i = log₂(1 + i). We set y_{n_o}, i.e., the relevance label at the index of the observed species (n_o), to one, and all other values of y zero. Consequently, for our problem, Eq. (1) simplifies to
l(s, y) = −∑_{j=1}^n log₂ c_{n_o, n_j}.
We set the highest rank up to which model predictions are evaluated (n) to 500.

**Species distribution models.** Single-species SDMs were built by relating occurrences and pseudoabsences to a subset of predictors selected from the low-resolution set. Three replicates of pseudoabsences (10,000) were generated with the geographic-specific approach and a background of environmentally stratified pseudoabsences. Species observations were thinned so that no more than one observation per 100 × 100 m grid cell was considered. Each species was modeled with an ensemble of different statistical techniques, using five model algorithms with specific parameterizations: generalized linear models (GLMs) were implemented with second-order polynomials and logit link function; generalized additive models were implemented with thin-plate regression splines with maximum of four degrees of freedom, and logit link function; gradient boosting machines were implemented with 5000 trees, an interaction depth of two, a learning rate of 0.001, and a Bernoulli distribution; random forests were implemented with 5000 trees and a minimum node size of one; and maximum entropy models were implemented with all feature types. For all algorithms, the total weights of occurrences and pseudoabsences were set to be equal in model calibrations and models were replicated three times with the different sets of pseudoabsences. Ensemble predictions were derived by averaging the probabilistic predictions of all combinations of the five algorithms times three replicates. Species distribution modeling was conducted in the R environment, using the packages mgcv, randomForest, gbm, and dismo.

For each taxon, we used a variable-selection procedure to choose the best predictors. We tested all feasible combinations of predictors by running GLMs with second-degree polynomials and equal sums of weights for occurrences and pseudoabsences, and ranked them based on adjusted explained deviance. Feasible combinations of predictors thereby were defined based on two criteria. Firstly, all absolute pairwise Pearson correlations within a predictor set had to be lower than 0.7, so that collinearity was contained within the tolerable range. Secondly, the number of predictors was fixed for each taxon, and varied between three and ten as a function of the number of occurrences in the calibration dataset (26–35: three predictors; 36–45: four predictors; 46–55: five predictors; 56–65: six predictors; 66–75: seven predictors, 76–85: eight predictors, 86–95: nine predictors; and >95: ten predictors). This second criterion was implemented to achieve an approximate presence-to-predictor ratio ≥10. Taxa with 8–25 occurrences were modeled using ensembles of small models, using the three best predictors selected from the variable selection procedure. Taxa with less than eight occurrences were not modeled. For species distribution modeling, we used the low-resolution training data set as described above, but given the spatial thinning applied, data was only sufficient for consider 2465 of the 2477 species modeled with DNNs.

### Predictive analysis
**Assessment of overall performance.** We validated the predictions of multispecies DNNs and stacked SDMs (SSDMs) on citizen science observations and survey data. All trained model configurations were evaluated against the held-out test set of the InfoFlora observations, comparing ranks of observed species in the predictions, and top-1, top-5, top-10, and top-20 accuracies. As we were interested in the performance of typical field observations and our test set was taxonomically stratified, we calculated weighted means of these statistics, using taxon-wise numbers of training observations as weights. We validated both the weighted (after the first 50 training epochs with taxonomically stratified sampling) and the unweighted (after full training) versions of multispecies DNNs. We compared the predictive performance for 2465 taxa for which multispecies DNN and SSDMs predictions existed with five observations per taxon, resulting in 12,325 observations. We tested for significant differences between models using two-sided, paired Wilcoxon tests and the Holm correction for multiple comparisons as implemented in the R package rstatix.

Multispecies DNNs with the full set of predictors and SSDMs were also evaluated against data from the BDM survey which were not used for training. To this end, we first generated probabilistic predictions for each cell in the taxon-by-site presence/absence matrix. To do so for multispecies DNNs, we first computed observation probability (i.e., applying the softmax function to the raw model predictions) for each day from April to September. Then, we summarized these predictions by their 90th percentile. We used the 90th percentile rather than the maximum, in order to have a robust estimator. Next, for each taxon individually (i.e., for each column), we calculated the area under the receiver operating curve (AUC), indicating the capacity of the models to discriminate between locations where species are present and locations where species are absent. This assessment was based on 1345 taxa which were present in the test data. Similarly, we calculated AUC for each site individually (i.e., for each row), indicating the capacity of the models to discriminate species present at a site from species absent at a site. Site-by-site AUC was calculated based on the predictions for all 2465 taxa for which multispecies DNN and SSDMs predictions existed, including those that were never observed in the BDM survey.

**Timing of seasonal maxima in observation probability.** We summarized seasonal observation probabilities pixel-wise by the timing of their maxima (t_p,max). For each pixel, we first predicted observation probabilities of the selected species with the high-resolution DNN for each day from March to September. Then, we smoothed the seasonal response curves with a running mean, using the AvgPool1d function of the torch module with a kernel size of 22 days. The curves were smoothed with running means of roughly three weeks to remove possible short-term fluctuations while still being flexible enough to capture seasonal peaks. Short-term fluctuations existed in rare cases in uncommon habitats, such as steep, rocky slopes, especially when observation probabilities for the focal species were comparably low. Next, we identified the modes of the smoothed response curves, and extracted the timing of the highest mode. Finally, we masked pixels with smoothed maxima below 0.01 and those for which no modes could be identified, as these pixels were assumed to be unsuitable for the species. For the nationwide maps, timings of peak observation probability were aggregated to 200 m spatial resolution by median.

We evaluated how well t_p,max corresponded to the date of Inflora observations for which observed individuals were reported to be in full bloom. For a fraction of InfoFlora observations information on phenology state existed, most of which reporting the category 'full bloom' for the observed individuals. We evaluated phenology for the common forbs Ajuga reptans, Aster bellidiastrum, Galium pumilum agg., Listera ovata, Orchis mascula, and Taraxacum officinale agg., as well as for all other species with at least 10,000 training observations and 30 observations with phenology information. To this end, we filtered observations with phenology state based on the same criteria as high-resolution training observations and matched them with predictions of t_p,max as described above. Then, we calculated the median difference between t_p,max and observation date (bias), and Spearman rank correlation to measure how well the predicted spatial patterns of t_p,max correspond to observed patterns. Note that although we considered observations for validation that were also used for training, we evaluated the predictions for an unseen attribute (phenological state). Moreover, with 0.5% median, species-specific fraction of training observations used for phenology validation was minor and thus had a negligible effect on the model fit.

**Mapping potentially dominant tree species.** In order to map potentially dominant tree species, we first identified pixels with land cover classes including trees and shrubs, i.e., 'normal dense forests', 'forest stripes, edges', 'forest fresh cuts', 'devastated forests', 'open forests (on agricultural areas)', 'open forest (on unproductive areas)', 'brush forest', 'groves, hedges', 'clusters of trees (on agricultural areas)', 'trees in unproductive areas', 'scrub vegetation', and 'unproductive grass and shrubs', as defined by ref. 82. For each of these wooded pixels, we then predicted daily observation probabilities for the species distinguished by the Swiss forest vegetation database from February to November and averaged them. Then, we quantified taxonomic reporting bias for each species as the ratio between summed cover-abundance percentages across the surveys of the Swiss forest vegetation database and the corresponding summed observation probabilities, and corrected observation probabilities for all pixels and species by multiplying the raw observation probabilities with these bias estimates. Finally, for each pixel, we identified the potentially dominant species as the one with the highest bias-corrected observation probability. For the nationwide maps, potentially dominant species were aggregated to 200 m spatial resolution by mode. As a sensitivity analysis, we generated equivalent maps for the northern Ticino region based on the low-resolution NDCG DNN and SSDMs.

We validated predicted potentially dominant tree taxa against observed ones based on 1867 plots from the National Forest Inventory. As the coordinates of the NFI sites fell exactly on the corners of four pixels of our prediction layer, we extracted reweighted observation probabilities of all adjacent pixels and averaged them, before identifying the potentially dominant taxon. We evaluated prediction success for dominance predictions of both, raw observation probabilities, as well as for the bias-corrected observation probabilities, using top-1 accuracy to measure overall prediction success and F1 score to estimate performance for individual species.

**Projecting observation probabilities into the future.** In order to project observation probabilities to 2060, we first estimated future climatic conditions. To this end, we deduced expected deviations in temperature and precipitation for 2060 from the CH2018 projections of the National Centre for Climate Services. In the region of the evaluated gradient, these projections estimated an increase in temperatures of about 2.5°C and 1.75°C for summer and winter respectively, a decrease in summer precipitation of about 17.5% and an increase in winter precipitation of about 7.5% for the emission scenario RCP4.5. We updated the corresponding low-resolution variables with these changes, and used a univariate generalized additive model with thin-plate regression splines to estimate future frost change frequency as a function of future winter temperature. The other low-resolution predictors were assumed to remain constant. We then predicted observation probabilities of potentially dominant graminoid species under current and future conditions, for May first, June first, July first, and August first, and applied a taxonomic-reporting-bias correction as described above but using survey data from the dry meadows and pastures initiative. For plotting, we smoothed the bias-corrected curves of observation probability along the gradient, using local polynomial regression fitting (R function loess) with a span of 0.15. As a sensitivity analysis, we also mapped bias-corrected habitat suitability based on SDM ensembles along the gradient under current conditions. Unless otherwise noted, model predictions were made in Python, while subsequent analyses as well as mapping were done in the R environment, using the packages ROCR, terra, mgcv, reticulate, RColorBrewer, and magick.

---

# Elucidating governing factors of PFAS removal by polyamide membranes using machine learning and molecular simulations

## Abstract
Per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFASs) have recently garnered considerable concerns regarding their impacts on human and ecological health. Despite the important roles of polyamide membranes in remediating PFAS-contaminated water, the governing factors influencing PFAS transport across these membranes remain elusive. In this study, we investigate PFAS rejection by polyamide membranes using two machine learning (ML) models, namely XGBoost and multimodal transformer models. Utilizing the Shapley additive explanation method for XGBoost model interpretation unveils the impacts of both PFAS characteristics and membrane properties on model predictions. The examination of the impacts of chemical structure involves interpreting the multimodal transformer model incorporated with simplified molecular input line entry system strings through heat maps, providing a visual representation of the attention score assigned to each atom of PFAS molecules. Both ML interpretation methods highlight the dominance of electrostatic interaction in governing PFAS transport across polyamide membranes. The roles of functional groups in altering PFAS transport across membranes are further revealed by molecular simulations. The combination of ML with computer simulations not only advances our knowledge of PFAS removal by polyamide membranes, but also provides an innovative approach to facilitate data-driven feature selection for the development of high-performance membranes with improved PFAS removal efficiency.

## Introduction
Per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFASs) are anthropogenic compounds that possess a fluorinated carbon backbone and varying functional head groups. The strong carbon-fluorine bonds and helical conformation that shields the redox-reactive carbons on the inside lead to exceptional stability of PFASs with high resistance to water, oil, and heat. These unique properties make PFASs suitable for a broad range of commercial applications, such as nonstick cookware, stain-resistance products, and flame retardants, but the persistence, toxicity, and bioaccumulative potential of PFASs have garnered considerable concerns regarding their impacts on both human and ecological health. The health concerns on long-chain PFAS, such as perfluorooctane sulfonic acid (PFOS) and perfluorooctanoic acid (PFOA), have led to gradual phase-out in long-chain PFAS production within North America and Europe, which is accompanied by a shift towards higher productions of shorter-chain alternatives. However, recent investigations also reveal toxicity associated with short-chain PFAS compounds due to their similar persistence and higher mobility compared to their long-chain counterparts.

The ubiquitous presence of PFASs in global water resources has raised alarm as reported by numerous studies. It has been suggested that PFAS-contaminated drinking water is one of the major pathways of PFAS exposure to humans, resulting from ineffective PFAS removal by conventional water treatment processes. Recently, the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (USEPA) proposed the final National Primary Drinking Water Regulation for six PFAS species, setting extremely low maximum contamination levels (MCLs) for PFOA and PFOS at 4 ng L⁻¹ as well as other PFASs including perfluorohexane sulfonic acid (PFHxS), perfluoroonanoic acid (PFNA), and GenX at 10 ng L⁻¹. Such stringent regulations, along with the threat of an expanding list of PFASs with regulatory implications, warrant the need to develop effective technologies for PFAS removal. Such technologies are vital for the potential upgrade of water treatment facilities to meet the corresponding regulations.

Membrane separation represents an important approach of treating PFAS-contaminated water sources. Previous studies have evaluated the efficiencies of PFAS removal by nanofiltration (NF) and reverse osmosis (RO), in which a dense polyamide membrane with subnanometer pores is used to allow faster and selective transport of water over PFAS. While several studies have applied computational chemistry techniques to predict PFAS degradation and adsorption, to the best of our knowledge, no effective approaches for predicting membrane separation performance in PFAS removal, which is essential for rational membrane selection and design, have been reported in the literature. Such a knowledge gap is largely due to the lack of a mechanistic understanding of PFAS interactions with polyamide membranes at both the water-membrane interface and the molecular level. Despite the importance of membrane and PFAS properties in governing PFAS transport, a fundamental property-structure-performance relationship has not been established for PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. Various PFAS compounds, which are well beyond the well-studied PFOS and PFOA, may vary in functional group substitution, ionization state, chain length, and other characteristics. The diverse properties of PFASs, coupled with the existence of membranes with varied perm-selectivity, make the identification of key factors governing PFAS removal in membrane treatment a particularly complex and challenging task. Predictive models that can accurately estimate the efficiency of PFAS removal while unveiling the influences of membrane and molecular properties on PFAS transport across polyamide membranes are critical. Such models not only contribute to the advancement of knowledge but also facilitate wide adoption of polyamide NF and RO membranes for the remediation of PFAS-contaminated waters.

In recent years, machine learning (ML) algorithms have been increasingly applied to predicting membrane performance and investigating the governing mechanisms of membrane separation. Thanks to its powerful capability of leveraging large datasets, ML can uncover intricate relationships among multiple variables. While ML has been successfully used to forecast the efficiency of polyamide NF and RO membranes in removing organic micropollutants, its applications to PFAS removal are very limited. Furthermore, ML models predicting membrane performance in the literature have predominantly relied on molecular descriptors, such as molecular weight and logarithm of n-octanol/water partition coefficient (log K_ow), while direct utilization of chemical structures as input variables has been rarely explored. The incorporation of chemical structure in ML models can provide a more in-depth understanding of how specific structural features influence the behavior of PFAS transport during membrane filtration. However, integrating information of chemical structure into ML models remains a significant challenge given the diverse molecular configurations of PFAS compounds.

Transformer neural network, initially developed for natural language processing (NLP), has been successfully applied to computer vision and material design. Unlike traditional neural networks, transformers apply a novel mechanism known as "attention", which allows them to selectively prioritize input variables during model training. This distinct mechanism enables transformers to effectively analyze and identify the interdependencies between variables, highlighting the influences of individual elements within a sequence. By utilizing simplified molecular input line entry system (SMILES) strings to represent the intricate chemical structures of PFAS compounds, the robust capabilities of transformers in analyzing sequential data offer a promising approach to exploring the impacts of chemical structures on the transport of PFASs across polyamide membranes.

In this study, we employ XGBoost and multimodal transformer models to elucidate the performance and underlying mechanisms of PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. Using training data collected from existing literature sources, the predictive performances of the ML models are evaluated with our experimental data obtained from NF treatment of aqueous film forming foam (AFFF)-contaminated feedwater. Given that AFFF contains a diverse set of PFAS compounds, evaluating the ML models with this PFAS mixture serves as a robust method to validate the accuracy and generalizability of models in predicting membrane performance for PFAS removal. To investigate the PFAS transport mechanisms with XGBoost model, we employ Shapley additive explanation (SHAP), a model interpretation method rooted in the cooperative game theory, to analyze the key governing factors influencing PFAS removal by polyamide membranes. We then introduce a novel methodology utilizing a multimodal transformer model to investigate the role of chemical structure in governing PFAS removal through heat maps, which visualize the contributions of individual chemical elements to the model prediction. The integrated interpretation applied to XGBoost and multimodal transformer models provides macroscopic insights related to membrane and PFAS properties, as well as microscopic perspectives pertaining to chemical structure. This approach enhances our understanding of PFAS removal by offering a comprehensive analysis at both levels. The molecular mechanisms underlying PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes unveiled by the ML models were further probed by molecular dynamics (MD) simulations. We conclude with a discussion on the implications of our findings to the use of NF and RO as competitive and vital technologies to achieve efficient remediation of PFAS-contaminated waters.

## Results and discussion
### Prediction performance of ML models
The XGBoost and multimodal transformer models were trained with 457 data points collected from existing literature sources, which include PFAS removal pertaining to 21 PFAS species and 16 types of polyamide NF and RO membranes. The model performance was subsequently assessed using 77 data points obtained from our own experiments. By evaluating the model accuracy with a complex mixture of PFASs from AFFF-contaminated feedwater, we demonstrated the performance of the ML models under a more challenging condition (than using individual PFAS). The predictive accuracy was examined using root mean squared error (RMSE) and mean absolute error (MAE). As shown in Fig. 2, the XGBoost model has a RMSE of 8.4% and a MAE of 6.4%, while RMSE and MAE of the multimodal transformer model are 5.9% and 4.5%, respectively. Although the multimodal transformer model shows slightly better predictive accuracy, such minor differences suggest that these models are comparable in their predictive performance. Compared to previous studies using ML to estimate membrane performance (RMSE of 4.6–17.5%; MAE of 6.1–13.2%), the XGBoost and multimodal transformer models in this study exhibited comparable or superior predictive accuracy. Expanding the dataset to include a broader range of PFAS removal rates and standardizing experimental protocols across studies are crucial to enhance model accuracy and reliability further. Additionally, as shown by our recent publications, ML models with strong predictive performance can adequately capture solute transport mechanisms. Accordingly, we evaluate the potential of our ML models to reveal the governing factors of PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes in the next sections.

### Identifying key factors for PFAS removal by the SHAP method
Although ML techniques have been increasingly applied in the fields of environmental science and engineering, the black box nature of ML makes it challenging to understand the decision-making process. Explainable artificial intelligence (XAI) has been used to explore the contributions of input variables and the underlying knowledge of ML models, enhancing the trust and confidence of the model predictions. In this section, we implemented the SHAP method, a type of XAI based on the cooperative game theory, to elucidate the knowledge of the XGBoost model regarding the governing mechanisms of PFAS removal by polyamide membranes.

As shown in Fig. 3a, the SHAP summary plot illustrates the influence of each input variable on the model prediction. The y-axis arrangement indicates the hierarchical importance of the input variable, with importance descending from the top to the bottom. The colors of dots, combined with their SHAP values, demonstrate the correlations of input variables with their SHAP values. To further quantify these SHAP values, we calculated the average of absolute SHAP values of all data points (i.e., the SHAP importance score). As presented in Fig. 3b, the input variables with the largest contributions to the model predictions (i.e., the highest SHAP importance scores) are charge product (whose definition is described in the Methods section), molecular weight cut-off (MWCO), log K_ow, and initial concentration of PFAS (C_in). Considering the membrane separation mechanisms related to those input variables, electrostatic interaction, which is directly represented by the charge product, is the most important separation mechanism that determines the model predictions of PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes, followed by size exclusion (manifested by MWCO) and adsorption (manifested by log K_ow and C_in).

Although the SHAP importance score reveals the dominant factors that possess the highest weights to determine the model predictions, the correlation of each input variable with the model output, which is essential to understanding whether the knowledge learned by ML models aligns with the domain knowledge, is still unclear. Hence, we further probed the impacts of the four top-ranked input variables (i.e., charge product, MWCO, log K_ow, and C_in) using SHAP dependence plots, which elucidate how the SHAP value changes as a function of each variable. As shown in Fig. 3c, the SHAP values increase with charge product (i.e., an increase of charge product leads to an increase of PFAS removal rate). Due to the acidic functional groups (e.g., carboxyl and sulfonate groups) and strong electronegativity of fluorine, most PFASs exhibit low pKa values (typically pKa <1). Therefore, PFASs are subject to nearly complete deprotonation and carry negative charges under most experimental pH conditions (pH of 3.5–10.0) from which the PFAS removal data were collected. Except for perfluorooctane sulfonamide (FOSA), electrostatic repulsion (i.e., charge product >0) exists between the PFASs and the membrane surface, due to the negative charge of polyamide membranes as a result of carboxyl groups generated from hydrolysis of the unreacted acyl chloride –C(=O)Cl groups. As electrostatic repulsion between the negatively charged PFASs and membrane surface gets stronger (an increase of charge product), a higher removal rate of PFAS is correctly predicted by the XGBoost model (higher SHAP values).

The contribution of MWCO to the model prediction is presented in Fig. 3d. The decrease of MWCO leads to an increase of SHAP values, indicating that lower PFAS removal rates are predicted by the ML model for membranes with higher MWCO values, which are typically related to larger membrane pore sizes. This result is consistent with the mechanism of size exclusion for membrane separation. For example, the data points with low SHAP values (e.g., less than −4.2, indicating low PFAS removal) are associated with membranes with the highest MWCO (e.g., the XN45 membrane), which are known as loose polyamide NF membranes that have limited solute-retaining capability.

The third- and fourth-ranked variables are log K_ow and the initial concentration of PFAS (C_in), both of which are related to adsorption. Figure 3e shows the influence of log K_ow on the model predictions. Except for a small fraction (1.8%) of outliers, higher SHAP values were generally observed as a result of an increase of log K_ow, which corresponds to a higher tendency of adsorption. As reported in the literature, adsorption of PFASs to the membrane surface contributes to their removal by NF membranes, especially at the early stage of membrane filtration. Also, the binding kinetics for long-chain PFASs are faster than short-chain PFASs due to their stronger hydrophobic interactions with membranes. While log K_ow primarily represents the hydrophobicity of compounds, it is also an indirect indicator of PFAS size that is positively correlated to PFAS removal rate based on size exclusion. As the chain length of PFAS species increases, its hydrophobic nature typically gets stronger, leading to a higher log K_ow. This intertwined relationship between molecular size and hydrophobicity could be attributed when explaining the correlation between SHAP value and log K_ow.

Despite a high contribution of C_in to the model predictions, we did not observe a clear relationship between C_in and SHAP values. This lack of trend might be due to a broad range of C_in used in the literature for our dataset. Although a majority (~70%) of the data points are associated with low PFAS concentrations of less than 20 μM, ~30% of data points involve higher C_in up to higher than 9 mM. Given such a skewed data distribution, it is challenging for the ML model to capture a clear, linear correlation of C_in with the SHAP value across the entire range. Even though, the XGBoost model is still able to capture the impact of C_in from several small portions of the data range without compromising the model performance due to the nature of gradient boosting tree.

### Probing key factors for PFAS removal by transformer model
Although the SHAP approach provides essential insights into the key factors that govern PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes, it remains elusive how chemical features of PFASs influence their transport across polyamide membranes. To bridge this knowledge gap, we implemented multimodal transformer model, which is known for its effectiveness of processing sequential data like SMILES strings. The heat-map visualization of the attention score of each atom from the multimodal transformer model enables us to identify specific atoms (and the corresponding functional groups) that have the highest importance in model predictions. Based on the functionality of PFAS species, the heat maps were grouped into perfluoroalkyl carboxylic acids (PFCA), perfluoroalkyl sulfonic acids (PFSA), and other PFASs, where the colors of the atoms depict the values of attention scores.

As shown by the heat maps of PFCA, the oxygen atoms of the carbonyl group (as part of the carboxyl group) exhibit the highest attention scores across all the PFCA compounds, regardless of the chain length. Similarly, the sulfur atom within the sulfonate group exhibited the highest attention scores for PFSA compounds. The particular functional groups associated with those atoms displaying the highest attention scores deprotonate at the experimental pH and result in a negative charge, which largely governs the electrostatic interactions of PFASs with the membrane surface. In contrast, carbon and fluorine atoms tend to exhibit lower attention scores. Therefore, in alignment with the insights revealed by the SHAP method, the multimodal transformer model consistently identifies electrostatic interaction as the predominant mechanism influencing the model prediction of polyamide NF and RO membrane performance.

The heat maps for other PFAS compounds than PFCA and PFSA, including perfluoro-3-methoxypropanoic acid (PFMOPA), perfluoro-4-methoxybutanoic acid (PFMOBA), 2,3,3,3-tetrafluoro-2-(heptafluoropropoxy)propanoic acid (GenX), 1H,1H,2H,2H-perfluorohexane sulfonic acid (4:2 FTS), 1H,1H,2H,2H-perfluorooctane sulfonic acid (6:2 FTS), and FOSA, are shown in Fig. 4c. For PFMOPA, PFMOBA, and GenX, the oxygen atoms associated with the carbonyl group exhibit the highest attention scores, suggesting that the dominant role of electrostatic interaction in regulating PFAS removal also applies to those carboxyl-containing PFAS species. Interestingly, the oxygen atoms representing the ether (–O–) group also show high attention scores for these PFAS species. The presence of an ether group increases the hydrophilicity of PFASs and thus alters their interactions with the membrane. Also, the heat maps of 4:2 FTS and 6:2 FTS, both of which contain a sulfonate group, demonstrate a similar trend to that of PFSA. In these cases, the sulfur atom shows the strongest influence on the model predictions. However, the highest attention score of FOSA is attributed to the fluorine atoms at the center of the compound. Unlike other PFAS compounds investigated in this study, the pKa value of FOSA is close to neutral pH due to the presence of sulfonamide group. This leads to a reduced degree of deprotonation and consequently, much weaker electrostatic interaction between FOSA and membrane surface. Thus, multimodal transformer model adeptly identifies functional distinctions between FOSA and other PFAS compounds, uniquely prioritizing the estimation of compound size. This estimation, indicated by the distance between the compound's center and head group, emerges as a pivotal chemical feature for predicting FOSA removal.

We further quantify the influence of chemical structure on PFAS removal by calculating the relative percentage of attention score (out of the total attention score summed for all the atoms) for each functional group. Compared to the carboxyl group of PFCA with the same chain length (excluding the carbon in the functional group), the sulfonate group of PFSA generally has a higher attention score percentage. Although both PFSA and PFCA are easily deprotonated, the pKa values of PFSA are lower than those of PFCA, suggesting more negative molecular charge and consequently stronger electrostatic interactions with membrane surfaces for PFSA. Such an effect is further supported by the atomic charge (i.e., the distribution of electron density across individual atoms in a molecule) of PFCA and PFSA. Compared to the oxygen atoms of carboxyl groups, the oxygen atoms of sulfonate groups exhibit more negative atomic charges, indicating stronger electrostatic interactions with membrane surfaces. Thus, the multimodal transformer model discerned such differences in PFAS-membrane interactions when making predictions of PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. Additionally, for other PFAS species than PFCA and PFSA, the functional groups pertaining to the atom where the atomic charge is the most negative generally match those with the highest attention scores from the multimodal transformer model (except for FOSA), as shown in Fig. 4 and Supplementary Fig. 6.

Therefore, both ML models (i.e., the XGBoost model and multimodal transformer model) reveal unequivocally that functional groups relating to electrostatic interactions are the major structural features that govern the removal of most PFASs by polyamide NF and RO membranes. The roles of functional groups in regulating PFAS transport across polyamide membrane will be further explored by computer simulations in the next section.

### Mechanisms of PFAS transport revealed by MD simulations
MD simulations were performed to probe the molecular mechanisms of PFAS transport across a charged and crosslinked piperazine (PIP)-trimethyl chloride (TMC) polyamide membrane. We selected three PFASs including PFHxA, GenX, and PFPeS that contain different functional groups (carboxyl, carboxyl+ether, and sulfonic) with identical carbon chain lengths. Due to their low pKa values, ionized PFAS molecules were used in our simulations. The potential of mean force (PMF) profiles were obtained by umbrella sampling simulations followed by the weighted histogram analysis method (WHAM) to probe the differences between the PFAS transport. Additionally, the PMF of transporting a water molecule was calculated for comparison. Initially, the PMF decreases as PFAS moves from bulk water towards the polyamide membrane (|z| from 5 to 3.5 nm), denoting spontaneous adsorption due to their hydrophobic character (e.g., Liu et al.). The adsorption free energy is observed to be −12 to −9 kJ mol⁻¹. As the PFAS enters the dense layer of the polyamide membrane (|z| < 3.5 nm; dotted lines in Fig. 5b) and moves towards the center, the PMF increases, denoting exclusion free energy arising from size, charge, and dielectric exclusion effects. The exclusion free energy defined as the difference between maximum and minimum PMF is in the range of 55–65 kJ mol⁻¹. Both the adsorption and exclusion free energy contribute to PFAS rejection. The adsorption free energy presents as an energy barrier for PFAS to move from the polyamide membrane to permeate-side bulk water, whereas exclusion free energies prevent PFAS from entering the membrane. In comparison to PFAS, water does not show any adsorption and has an exclusion free energy of 5.7 kJ mol⁻¹. The higher energy barriers of PFAS molecules compared to water indicates that PFAS transport is significantly hindered, while water molecules can easily permeate through the membrane. Since the exclusion free energy of PFAS is much higher than its adsorption free energy, the former is more significant for PFAS rejection. These results are consistent with ML results that find charge product and MWCO is more significant for PFAS rejection than log K_ow.

The permeability and rejection trends of PFAS are dependent on the maximum PMF that reflects the energy barrier ΔG‡ associated with a PFAS reaching the center of the polyamide membrane. The ΔG‡ of PFAS is much higher than water and follows the order GenX ≤ PFPeS < PFHxA. To interpret the order of ΔG‡, van der Waals and electrostatic energies, total number of hydrogen bonds, average orientation of PFAS, and root-mean-squared-deviation of PFAS (a measure of conformational change) were calculated along the reaction coordinate. The increase in electrostatic energy as PFAS moves from bulk water toward the center of the membrane follows the same order as that of ΔG‡ (GenX < PFPeS < PFHxA). The change in the van der Waals (modeled using Lennard-Jones interactions) and electrostatic (modeled using Coulombic interactions) energies along the reaction coordinates demonstrates that change in electrostatic interactions is more dominant than van der Waals. Since the PFAS molecules have the same net charge, the difference in electrostatic energies indicates the influence of dipole-dipole and dipole-charge interactions between PFAS and polyamide membrane. This implies that electrostatic attraction from dipole-dipole and dipole-charge interactions between PFAS and the polyamide membrane lowers the energy barrier created by electrostatic repulsion. While the input variables of ML models do not contain dipole properties of PFAS, higher attention scores in carboxyl, sulfonate, and ether groups indicate that the importance of electronegative atoms, which play a significant role in dipole interactions, are captured by the models to a certain degree. The order of van der Waals interaction energy is opposite to that of ΔG‡ and much lower in magnitude than electrostatic energy. The fluctuations in the number of hydrogen bonds of PFAS along the reaction coordinate z negatively correlated with the fluctuations in the PMF, indicating that dehydration of PFAS is likely to play a role in regulating PFAS rejection. The total number of hydrogen bonds lost by PFAS as it moves from bulk water (|z| > 5 nm) to the center of the membrane (|z| < 3.5 nm) is shown in Figs. 5c–f. The number of hydrogen bonds lost is indicative of PFAS dehydration and follows the order of PFHxA < GenX < PFPeS. Such an order does not follow the order of ΔG‡ but matches that of log K_ow of PFAS because higher lipophilicity leads to higher propensity of PFAS dehydration. Therefore, while dehydration of PFAS might influence the membrane rejection performance, it is not as influential as the effects of electrostatic and size exclusion. This result is also consistent with ML results where log K_ow was found to be less influential than parameters associated with electrostatic and size exclusion (e.g., charge product and MWCO). The PFAS molecules exhibited significant changes in orientation and conformation near the center of the membrane, which did not correspond to fluctuations in the PMF. We attribute these changes to the tortuous nature of the pore pathways in the polyamide membrane.

### Environmental implications
In this study, we employed XGBoost and multimodal transformer models to elucidate the governing factors of PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. Both XGBoost and multimodal transformer models exhibited high prediction accuracy (with RMSE and MAE values of 6–8% and 5–6%, respectively), effectively correlating multiple variables (including membrane and PFAS properties as well as operational conditions) with membrane efficiency of PFAS rejection. The XGBoost model, interpreted with the SHAP method, and the multimodal transformer model, incorporating SMILES strings, consistently highlight the importance of electrostatic interaction in governing PFAS removal during NF and RO processes. MD simulations results agree well with ML results in identifying electrostatic interaction as a major governing factor for PFAS removal by polyamide membrane. Also, MD simulations confirm the importance of functional groups in regulating PFAS transport. Specifically, MD simulations demonstrate that electrostatic attraction from dipole interactions reduces the electrostatic exclusion effects. Additionally, PFAS dehydration (loss of hydrogen bonds) is identified as one important factor for PFAS rejection.

The ML model interpretation plays a pivotal role in unraveling the complex mechanisms behind predictive analytics. In this study, ML model interpretation methods, including the SHAP method for the XGBoost model and heat maps highlighting the attention scores for the multimodal transformer model, unveil the underlying mechanisms of PFAS transport, based on which the performance predictions of ML models are determined. On one hand, the SHAP method for interpreting the XGBoost model reveals the general and macroscopic insights on how numerical values representing material properties and operational conditions govern the efficiency of NF and RO in removing PFASs. On the other hand, the multimodal transformer model integrated with molecular structures using SMILES strings provides a microscopic, molecular-level understanding of the detailed influences by chemical features of PFASs. This dual approach not only enhances the trust of the ML models but also promotes mechanistic knowledge of PFAS transport across polyamide membranes. The application of ML model interpretation methods, as demonstrated by the current study, can transform ML algorithms from simple predictive models into powerful tools of understanding and discovering complex governing factors behind the performance of membrane separation.

Furthermore, the results of this study, which identify key chemical features governing PFAS transport, have the potential to inform rational feature selection for membrane fabrication and optimization. For example, the highest importance of charge product and charged functional groups, as discerned by the interpretation of XGBoost and transformer models respectively, indicate that controlling electrostatic interactions between PFASs and membrane should be a prioritized strategy of promoting membrane selectivity to charged PFASs. Such knowledge attained by the ML models aligns with the outcomes of MD simulations, which demonstrates that PFAS molecules with the same carbon number but different functional groups experience varied energy barriers when traversing through polyamide membrane. Enhancing the electrostatic interactions by surface modification or the use of new monomers in interfacial polymerization is a promising direction of fabricating novel membrane materials with improved efficiency of PFAS removal. For instance, grafting materials with strong negative charge or using monomers carrying negatively charged moieties in interfacial polymerization has the potential of increasing electrostatic repulsion between PFAS and the membrane surface. Additionally, using monomers with lower dipole moments (e.g., melamine and pentaerythritol) may reduce the influence of electrostatic attraction from dipole interactions. We acknowledge that the majority of PFASs in our database are anionic species in nature. This is largely due to the lack of commercial standards for cationic, zwitterionic, and most non-ionic PFASs, and thus current mass-spectrometry based methods for PFAS quantification can only target anionic PFASs in most cases. Nevertheless, the ML methods presented in this study can be employed beyond anionic PFASs if their quantifications and corresponding removal efficiencies by polyamide NF and RO membranes are available in the future. Also, the high attention scores of ether group in determining model prediction suggest the importance of functional group substitution in PFAS transport across polyamide NF and RO membranes, which is also supported by the results of MD simulations. This highlights research needs of evaluating membrane performance for removing diverse PFASs species other than PFCAs and PFASs. In addition, with proper feature engineering to capture the distinct physical and chemical properties, the approaches developed in this work have the potential to predict the performance and gain mechanistic knowledge for other materials, such as novel membranes made of two-dimensional materials and nanochannels, as well as for the development of novel adsorbents and catalysts. Such applications enable the design of materials by leveraging an enhanced understanding of the intricate relationship between structural details and performance of functional materials.

## Methods
### Data collection
The data regarding PFAS rejection efficiencies of polyamide NF and RO membranes were collected from both the literature and our own experiments. For the data from the literature, which were used for ML model training, we extracted 457 data points either directly from tables presenting PFAS removal rates or by employing the Engauge Digitizer software to extract data from graphs. We also conducted experiments to evaluate the rejections of various PFASs from AFFF-contaminated feedwater using three commercial NF membranes and two modified NF membranes. From these experiments, 77 data points were acquired and used to assess the accuracy of ML models. The detailed membrane modification methods and the experimental procedure are described in the following sections.

### Materials
The AFFF sample was obtained from 3M Co., and the standard solution (mass-labelled PFAS extraction, MPFAC-24ES) for PFASs quantification was purchased from Wellington Laboratories Inc. Methanol (>99.9%, HPLC grade) was acquired from Honeywell. Sodium chloride (NaCl, ≥99.0%), 2-propanol (IPA, ≥99.5%), ethanol (≥99%), hydrochloric acid (HCl, 36%), sodium hydroxide (NaOH, ≥97.0%), ammonium hydroxide (NH₄OH, 28.0–30.0%), acrylic acid (CH₂CHCOOH, ≥99.0%), potassium persulfate (K₂S₂O₈, 99.0%), sodium bisulfite (Na₂S₂O₅, ≥58.5%), dopamine chloride, tri(hydroxymethyl) aminomethane (Tris, ≥99.8%), and poly(ethylenimine) (PEI, MW 800) were all purchased from Sigma-Aldrich. Deionized (DI) water (>18 MΩ·cm) was provided from a water purification system (Millipore). Commercial flat sheet NF membranes (NF90, NF270, and NF) were purchased from DuPont FILMTEC.

### Membrane surface modification
Membrane surface modification was performed on NF270 membrane to achieve different membrane surface properties. We selected NF270 membrane for surface modification due to its loose structure and expected low PFAS removal efficiency (so that changing membrane surface properties has the best potential to alter the PFAS removal rates). Before membrane surface modification, NF270 membranes were initially immersed in a 25 v/v% IPA aqueous solution for 30 min and then washed thoroughly with DI water to remove impurities, followed by storage in DI water at 4°C for further use. Two modification methods were employed to graft poly(acrylic acid) (PAA, a hydrophilic and negatively charged polymer) and poly(ethylenimine) (PEI, a hydrophilic and positively charged polymer) on the membrane surface. For the modification with PAA, a redox radical initiation reaction was employed, following the method reported by Belfer et al. Briefly, 1 M acrylic acid, 0.01 M K₂S₂O₈ and 0.01 M Na₂S₂O₅ solutions were prepared and poured onto the membrane surface to initiate the polymerization, which lasted for 1 h at the room temperature. The resulting PAA-modified membranes were then rinsed with DI water. For the modification with PEI, a dopamine-assisted grafting method was applied. In short, 0.16 mM dopamine chloride was added to a Tris buffer solution (10 mM, pH 8.5) and then poured onto the membrane surface. After 1 h of dopamine polymerization reaction, polydopamine (PDA)-deposited membranes were rinsed with DI water, followed by immersion in 10 g L⁻¹ of PEI aqueous solution for 4 h at room temperature. Finally, PEI-modified membranes were rinsed with DI water. After the modification steps, the PAA- and PEI-modified membranes were stored at 4°C until use.

### Membrane characterization
Several techniques were employed to characterize the surface properties of the commercial and modified membranes. Water contact angles of the membranes were measured with a contact angle goniometer (Krüss DSA 10, Hamburg, Germany) using the sessile drop method. An electrokinetic analyzer with an adjustable gap cell (SurPASS, Anton-Paar, Graz, Austria) was employed to measure the zeta-potential of the membrane surface. The zeta potential measurements were performed with 1 mM KCl as background electrolytes in the pH range of 2 to 10, which was adjusted by titration of 0.1 M HCl and 0.1 M NaOH solutions.

### The evaluation of PFAS rejections by NF membranes
The evaluation of PFAS rejections by the commercial and modified NF membranes was conducted in a lab-scale crossflow filtration system with an effective membrane area of 26.2 cm². The crossflow velocity was 21.3 cm s⁻¹ and the operating temperature was controlled at 25 ± 1°C. Before the tests, the filtration system was thoroughly cleaned with 1% NH₄OH in DI water and ethanol (1:1) solution. The membranes were compacted overnight with DI water, and the pure water permeate flux was then measured by a digital flowmeter (FlowCal 5000, Tovatech, Plano, TX) to determine the water permeability coefficients of the membranes. Subsequently, NaCl was added to achieve an ionic strength of 10 mM in the feedwater for the measurement of NaCl rejection, followed by the addition of AFFF into the feed reservoir (the sum of detected PFASs concentrations was ~157 μg L⁻¹) with care to avoid bubble formation. The pH of the feedwater was adjusted (to pH of 5, 7, or 9) using 1 M HCl and 1 M NaOH solutions. The feed and permeate samples were collected in 15 mL polypropylene tubes (Corning Inc., Corning, NY) after 1 h operation and then stored at 4°C until PFAS quantification.

### PFAS quantification
For sample preparation, 100 μL of the collected feed or permeate sample was mixed with 100 μL of internal standard solution (20 μg L⁻¹, MPFAC-24ES in methanol) in a polypropylene vial (VWR, Radnor, PA). The prepared samples were analyzed by an Agilent 1290 liquid chromatography paired with an Agilent 6460 triple quadrupole mass spectrometer (LC-QQQ-MS, Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA). The obtained concentrations were used for the calculation of PFAS rejection rates, which were used to evaluate the predictive performance of the ML models.

### Molecular properties of PFASs
The SMILES strings of the PFASs included in our data set were used to build the molecular structure in Avogadro software (version 1.2.0). Such structures were imported into Marvin View (version 22.22.0, Chemaxon, Budapest, Hungary) for calculating the values of pKa, maximum/minimum projection radius of PFAS molecule, and log K_ow. The computed values of the calculated molecular properties are listed in Supplementary Table 2 and Supplementary Data 1, with the obtained values being used as input variables for ML models. Additionally, atomic charges of the PFAS molecules were determined by the ORCA (version 5.0.3) software, and the dissociation of PFASs was considered when calculating the atomic charge of each atom. The atomic charge calculations were performed using the density functional theory method of B3LYP with def2-TZVPB basis set. The impact of the solvent (i.e., water) was computed by utilizing the Conductor-like Polarizable Continuum Model (CPCM), and the values of static dielectric constant and refractive index of water are 80.4 and 1.3, respectively. Finally, JANPA, an open-source JAVA software, was used for natural population analysis, which provides the value of atomic charge. RDKit, a Python library, was then used to visualize the atomic charge of each atom.

### Variable selection for ML models
We selected a set of variables pertaining to the properties of membranes and PFAS by considering membrane separation mechanisms such as size exclusion, Donnan exclusion, and adsorption. As a result, eight variables were selected including compound size, log K_ow, charge product (a product of membrane charge index and compound charge), water contact angle (WCA) and MWCO of the membranes, hydraulic pressure, C_in, and ionic strength (IS) of the feedwater. Especially, compound size and charge product were calculated using the following equations.
Compound size = (maximum projection radius × minimum projection radius)^0.5
Charge product = membrane charge index × compound charge
Membrane charge index = isoelectric point − pH
where the compound charge was calculated by weighting charged and neutral forms of PFAS at the experimental pH according to their respective proportions; the isoelectric points of the membranes were determined by identifying the pH at which the zeta potentials of membranes became zero. For the multimodal transformer model, SMILES strings were tokenized, a process in which the strings are converted into numerical values, and these numerical values were incorporated as additional input variables.

### ML models and their interpretation
We implemented two ML models, namely the XGBoost and multimodal transformer models, to predict PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. The selection of the XGBoost model was due to its strong performance in many data science competitions and our previous publications. While XGBoost has shown high predictive performance for structured and tabular data through its ensemble of decision trees, the multimodal transformer model leverages attention mechanisms, which enable efficient capturing of interdependencies especially in sequences like SMILES strings. Considering the potentially important roles of chemical structure on PFAS transport across membranes, the multimodal transformer model that incorporates SMILES strings representing structural information has the potential to unveil the impacts of chemical features on PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. To train the XGBoost and multimodal transformer models, we used 5-fold cross-validation and Bayesian optimization for hyperparameter tuning to avoid data leakage. Data were grouped according to experimental settings and combined with the corresponding PFAS names to create unique labels. Data sharing the same label were assigned to the same fold. This method ensured that data with similar experimental conditions (e.g., identical membrane type and PFAS, differing only in one variable) were kept within the same fold, effectively minimizing the risk of data leakage. After optimizing the hyperparameters, the ML models were trained using the entire training dataset with the optimal hyper-parameters. The models' predictive capabilities were then evaluated using our own experimental data on PFAS rejection. The performance of the models was evaluated by using RMSE and MAE. These metrics are mathematically expressed as follows:
RMSE = √(1/n ∑_{i=1}^n (y − ŷ)²)
MAE = 1/n ∑_{i=1}^n |y − ŷ|
where n is the number of samples, y represents the true values (i.e., the target labels of the dataset), ŷ represents the estimated values by the model.

After training the model, we employed SHAP, a commonly used XAI technique, to interpret the XGBoost model. In the SHAP method, the contribution of each variable to the model prediction is calculated by considering all possible combinations of variables and their corresponding contributions. Thus, the use of SHAP enabled us to unveil the key factors governing PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes. For the multimodal transformer model, SMILES strings were utilized as additional inputs during model training. The SMILES strings were tokenized into numerical values, with each numerical value representing a specific chemical element, to facilitate their incorporation into the model. To ensure uniformity, the length of the tokens was standardized based on the longest set of tokens observed in the PFAS compounds present within the dataset. If the length of tokens was shorter than the maximum length, zeros were padded to achieve the required length. During the training of multimodal transformer model, attention scores were extracted from each input variable across different layers of multiple attention heads. These scores were then averaged for each atom within the structure of PFAS compounds to create heat maps, which visually represent the importance of each atom in determining PFAS removal by polyamide NF and RO membranes.

### MD simulations of PFAS transport across polyamide membrane
**Membrane preparation.** Polyamide membrane composed of PIP and TMC was prepared with dimensions of 4.7 × 4.7 × 9 nm³. The PIP-TMC polyamide membrane was chosen as the representative NF membrane that is typically used in experiments. Compared to polyamide membranes fabricated using m-phenylene diamine (MPD) and TMC, the looser PIP-TMC polyamide membrane is also more likely to unveil the relation between the functional group and the rejection of PFAS. The cross-linked membranes are prepared following our previous work. The monomers, PIP and TMC, are randomly placed inside a simulation box with PIP:TMC molar ratio of 1.5 and a target density of 1.3 g cc⁻¹. The system of monomers is energy-minimized and simulated cross-linking is initiated under a canonical ensemble at 300 K. At each cross-linking step, the reactive sites of PIP and TMC were identified, and sites within specified cutoff distances of 0.45 nm were pulled towards each other using steered MD simulations (using virtual elastic springs). Once the reactive sites reached within 0.15 nm, physical amide bonds were assigned, appropriate H and Cl atoms were removed, and the topology of the molecule was updated. The cross-linking was continued until a cross-linking degree of 90% was achieved, which (excluding monomers) produced an O/N ratio of 1.14. At any intermediate cross-linking step if no reactive groups were found within the cutoff of 0.45 nm, the cutoff distance was increased by 0.05 nm. The monomeric TMC and PIP were removed and any remaining chloride atoms in TMCs were replaced with hydroxyl groups. Finally, one of the carboxyl groups in TMC containing exactly one amide bond was deprotonated. The charge density of our polyamide membrane is consistent with the charge density of membranes at pH 7.3 studied by Liu et al.

**Potential of mean force.** The crosslinked polyamide membrane was inserted at the center of a box of dimension 4.7 × 4.7 × 19 nm³, followed by the addition of PFAS at the position (2.35, 2.35, 0 nm) and solvation with TIP3P water. The Swissparam forcefield was applied to the polyamide membrane and PFAS. The system was energy minimized using the steepest descent, followed by canonical (NVT) ensemble for 50 ps, and isothermal-isobaric (NPT) ensemble for 20 ns. The temperature and pressure of the simulations were maintained at 300 K and 1 bar using a stochastic velocity rescaling thermostat with a time constant of 0.1 ps and Berendsen barostat with compressibility of 4.5 × 10⁻⁵ in z-direction (normal to the polyamide membrane) and 0 in x- and y-directions with a time constant of 10 ps. The initial configurations for umbrella sampling were generated by steering the PFAS molecules across the polyamide membrane with a force constant of 100 kJ mol⁻¹ nm⁻² and a pulling rate of 0.001 nm ps⁻¹. The reaction coordinate for umbrella sampling was defined as the displacement vector along the z-axis between the center of mass of PFAS and the polyamide membrane. A total of 120 windows were chosen for the reaction coordinate between ±6 nm for PIP-TMC. An umbrella potential with a force constant of 500 kJ mol⁻¹ nm⁻² was applied to PFAS in each window and equilibrated using eight cycles of 2.5 ns simulated annealing between 300 and 600 K and NPT equilibration of 5 ns. Finally, umbrella sampling simulations were performed with a force constant of 200 kJ mol⁻¹ nm⁻² using the Nosé-Hoover thermostat and Parrinello-Rahman barostat for 80 ns, until convergence in the PMF was achieved. Periodic boundary conditions were used in all directions. The PMF profiles were calculated with the weighted histogram analysis method (WHAM) using 300 bins, a tolerance of 10⁻⁸, and the periodic assumption. The integrated autocorrelation time was determined for each window to assign relative weights to the windows. All PMF calculations and analyses were performed using GROMACS 2022.

**Hydrogen bond analysis.** The hydrogen bonds formed between PFAS-polyamide membrane and PFAS-water were calculated using the MDAnalysis Python package. The oxygen, sulfur, and fluorine atoms were treated as acceptors and oxygen and nitrogen atoms with at least one bound hydrogen were treated as hydrogen bond donors. The cutoff distance between donor and acceptor atoms and the angle between donor-hydrogen-acceptor for hydrogen bonds were set at 0.35 nm and 150°, respectively. The total number of hydrogen bonds was determined as a function of the reaction coordinate from all umbrella sampling windows. The reaction coordinate is binned with a width of 0.05 nm and all data points in a bin are used to determine the average and standard deviation.

---

# Forecasting the eddying ocean with a deep neural network

## Abstract
Mesoscale eddies with horizontal scales from tens to hundreds of kilometers are ubiquitous in the upper ocean, dominating the ocean variability from daily to weekly time scales. Their turbulent nature causes great scientific challenges and computational burdens in accurately forecasting the short-term evolution of the ocean states based on conventional physics-driven numerical models. Recently, artificial intelligence (AI)-based methods have achieved competitive forecast performance and greatly increased computational efficiency in weather forecasts, compared to numerical models. Yet, their application to ocean forecasts remains challenging due to the different dynamic characteristics of the atmosphere and the ocean. Here, we develop WenHai, a data-driven eddy-resolving global ocean forecast system (GOFS), by training a deep neural network (DNN). The bulk formulae on momentum, heat, and freshwater fluxes are incorporated into the DNN to improve the representation of air-sea interactions. Ocean dynamics is exploited in the DNN architecture design to preserve ocean mesoscale eddy variability. WenHai outperforms a state-of-the-art eddy-resolving numerical GOFS and AI-based GOFS for the temperature profile, salinity profile, sea surface temperature, sea level anomaly, and near-surface current forecasts led by 1 day to at least 10 days. Our results highlight expertise-guided deep learning as a promising pathway for enhancing the global ocean forecast capacity.

## Introduction
The ocean is turbulent, containing eddies with a wide range of sizes. Among them, the mesoscale eddies form the major fraction of the ocean kinetic energy reservoir. These mesoscale eddies redistribute heat and materials in the ocean, contributing dominantly to the short-term (from daily to weekly time scales) variations of ocean thermohaline structures, regulating the primary productivity and further marine ecosystem, and acting as a major driver of extreme events like marine heatwaves. They also interact strongly with the overlaying atmosphere, influencing air temperature, humidity, winds, cloud fraction, and rainfall within local marine atmospheric boundary layer as well as atmospheric synoptic variability and large-scale circulations. Therefore, accurate eddy-resolving ocean forecasts are not only essential for supporting marine activities and managements but also necessary to improve weather forecast accuracy.

So far, ocean forecasts rely primarily on ocean general circulation models (OGCMs) that make forecasts by numerically discretizing and integrating the governing partial differential equations of the ocean. However, constructing an accurate eddy-resolving global ocean forecast system (GOFS) remains a challenging issue both computationally and scientifically. On the one hand, resolving mesoscale eddies requires a grid size of O(10 km) or even finer, imposing a massive computational burden for operating global-scale OGCMs especially for implementing advanced data assimilation and making ensemble forecast. In fact, the eddy-resolving numerical GOFS did not emerge until the recent decade partially due to the enlarged computational resources. On the other hand, the chaotic nature of mesoscale eddies makes their forecast very sensitive to errors in initial conditions, boundary forcings, and OGCMs. In particular, despite development over about a half-century, there are still many uncertainties in OGCMs, including numerical errors caused by discretization, uncertainties in parameterizations of unresolved processes, and insufficient representation of interactions of the ocean with other components of the Earth system.

Artificial intelligence (AI)-based methods provide a data-driven approach for making forecasts and have been successfully applied to the global medium-range weather forecasts.
